published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.

Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia.
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed ;
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.

A story of two “colonials.” Hicary was a
typiqal_ bash-Australian—a stockman and a good
horseman. He was riding this day towards
Drayton. He recognised the man who cantered
up behind him as Tyson, and said, “ Good-day.”
“Good-day,” was the reply. They rode along
together awhile. Tyson was talkative and in the
humor to joke. “What countryman are you?”
he asked. “A German,” untruthfully answered
the “Cornstalk.” “A German! Well, you’ve
the best seat I’ve ever seen on a horse for a Ger-
man.” “And what countryman are you?"
followed Hicary. “A native.” “A nativel
Well, you’ve the worst seat I’ve ever seen on a
horse for a native,”

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
“The Bulletin ” Scrap-Book
OLD LOVES.
Louise, have you forgotten yet
The corner of the flowery land,
The ancient garden where we met,
My hand that trembled in your hand?
Our lips foui d words scarce sweet enough,
As low beneath the willow trees
e sat; have you forgotten, love?
Do you remember, love, Louise 7
arie, have you forgotten yet
The loving barter that we made?
The rings we changed, the suns that set,
The woods fulfilled with sun and shade?
The fountains that were musical
By many an ancient trysting-tree—
Marie, have you forgotten all?
Do you remember, love, Marie?
Christine, do you remember yet
Your room with scents and roses gay?
My garret—near the sky ’tw.as set—
The April hours, the nights of May ?
The clear, calm nights—the stars above
That whispered they were fairest seen
Through no cloud-veil? Remember, kve 1
Do you remember, love, Christine?
Louise is dead, and, well-a-day 1
Malic a sadder path has ta'en ;
And pale Christine has passed away
In southern suns to bloom again.
Alas ! for one and all of us—
Marie, Louise, Christine, forget i
Our bower of love is ruinous,
And I alone remember yet.
From the French of Henri Murger.
Sydney Licensing Guardian exhorts the
publicans to oppose woman-suffrage. Woman,
being the rival, is the enemy of wine.
Labor troubles and free-labor irruptions
from Maoriland are proving slowly fatal to the
old-time shearing class in Queensland. Along
with the shearer many of the small Western towns
such as Aramac—are decaying also. The free-
laborer spends his pay in a distant country.
Man, chemically analysed, consists of
451bs. of carbon and nitrogen and five buckets of
water. Any man who is inclined to think too
much of himself, or to write poems about Ragged
Jane, or to damn Chicago, would do well to sit
down and seriously consider the fact that he con-
sists mostly of five buckets of water.
A Maoriland M.L.A. recently turned up
to address his constituents in an awful state of
liquor, and after looking at the audience with a
beery eye for some few minutes he lurched heavily
forward and fell off the platform on top of his
supporters. Then the crowd left unanimously,
and the proceedings closed with an M.H R. grop-
ing around on all-fours and stating feebly to the
table that he was in favor of “Prohib-hic-
bishern.”

The rabid prohibition nonconformist con-
science is a quaint institution in Maoriland.
There lives, down south, Jones, a young business
man guilty of the crime of owning a couple of
racehorses. In the same town resides the Rev.
Zedediah Catchmore, a clergyman whose efforts
in favor of prohibition have found much grace and
acceptance. The Rev. Zedediah wanted to let a
house of his, and placed it in the hands of an
agent. The ungodly Jones proposed ,to take it,
and was shown over the place by his reverence,
to whom he mentioned that he thought of putting
up some loose boxes oti the premises for his horses.
All seemed to go smoothly, and it only remained
for the rent to be named and the terms of tenancy
agreed’ upon; but very'shortly Jones received a
note, transcribed below verbatim
Jones, Esq. 28,12/’94.
Dear Sir,—l regret to say that in thinking over the
question of letting to you I fed that your connection with
the turf makes it impossible to me. lam satisfied that
the speculations of the racecourse are in violation of our
Lord’s golden rule, “Thou shalt love thy neighbour as
thyself,” and I must not, therefore, profit from such a
source. Regretting that I cannot oblige you as I would
so gladly do were no point of conscience involved,
I am yours faithfully,
Zedediah OatchmorK
Then Jones went to see the house-agent, in con-
versation with whom he said : “You know,
Smith, my wife was rather keen on the house, and
she was intending to use some of her own money
in paying part of the rent, if it was too stiff for
me.” The result of this conversation was dis-
closed next day when Jones received from Pastor
Catchmore the following indication of the docility
of a well-trained “ still small voice.” The would-
be landlord had by this time become acquainted
with Jones’s initials, and the cold “yours faith-
fully ” now blooms into “yours with regards ” :
Dear Sir,—Mr. Smith has explained to me that I shall
be virtually letting to Mrs. Jones. This satisfies my
: cruples, and if we can come to terms I shall be happy to
let to you either by the week or by lease, the latter being
perhaps the preferable method for both of us ; and I trust
you will find the house a very enjoyable home for your-
self and family. I am sending Mr. Smith my terms this
morning.
Yours with regards,
ZEDEDIAR CATCHMORR.
The terms were too stiff even for so holy a house,
and Jones is still pondering over the “ satisfaction
of the scruples.’’

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 16, 1895.
The Deaf Adder of Politics.
The Turner Government has undertaken to
devote part of the recess to considering its
position with regard to the Victorian Legis-
lative Council, and it is almost an open secret
that it intends to include the Referendum in
the pi’ogramme for next session as a remedy
for the present state of chronic deadlock.
As a peaceful, law-abiding Government this
is probably about as good a course as it could
be expected to take. A constitutional
Ministry ruling over a very small province is
not in a position to make a coup-iVetat unless
it has the whole population in a state of red-
hot enthusiasm at its back. Moreover, a
Ministry which consists very largely of
lawyers reared in the solemn atmosphere and
traditions of parchment and horse-hair and
6s. Bd., is not the material with which coups
d'etat are made.- Therefore George Turner
and his brilliant henchman, Isaac A. Isaacs,
are probably doing as good a thing as the
Constitution and the Premier’s peculiarly
legal cast of mind will allow under very hard
circumstances.
* # *
Unfortunately, the proposal to establish
the Referendum as a cure for the perennial
deadlock in Victorian politics is just as
adequate to the case as would be a pint of
water to extinguish Gehenna, and it is likely
to be fully an effective as a reproachful look
directed at a herd ol stampeded cattle. The
Bulletin says this with a perfect recollec-
tion of the fact that the Referendum is an
important part of its own political pro-
gramme, and a device which it has long and
consistently advocated. It believes as cor-
dially as ever that the proposed measure will
suit certain purposes better than anything
else that can be devised. As a guide-post by
which tjie nation can show its representatives
the way it wants them to go it has no equal;
but what is wanted in the present case is a
weapon by which the House of 48 Rich
Landlords can be scared out of its strongly
entrenched position, and a guide-post, re-
garded as an implement of offence, is of
little account.
« • •
Setting aside the fact that the Referendum
is avowedly intended as a weapon against
the Council, that it can’t be established with-
out the Council’s consent, and that the spec-
tacle of the Council consenting, unless under
extreme pressure—of which there is no sign
at present—is too picturesque for belief,
there remains the other fact that something
quite different is needed to meet the case.
The reason for the Referendum is that,
among the multitudinous issues brought
forward at a general election, it is impossible
for the people to express in any definite—
or even in any moderately indefinite
fashion their views on every important ques-
tion. Therefore, this law gives them a con-
venient means of instructing the men who re-
present them what to do or what not to do on
any given great issue. But, unfortunately,
it doesn't furnish them with any means of
instructing the men who don't represent
them. The popular veto is a handy device
by which the people can prevent Parliament
doing things of which they disapprove, but,
as the Victorian Legislative Council never
does anything, this hardly applies. That
body is a perpetual veto in itself. Also, the
power of initiative, by which the people car.
direct their representatives to proceed with
any proposed measure, only applies in cases
where the same body which gave the direc-
tion can discharge those legislators who fail
to comply with the popular will. The Vic
torian Council, however, can’t he discharged.
The people who would form the popular
majority in favor of a progressive measure,
and the people to whom the Council is even
nominally responsible, are two entirely
different constituencies The obstructive
body can’t be dissolved. It is duly aut horised
to go on its placid way, despite all the
Referendum on this earth, and even when it
expires the property qualification of its mem-
bers prevents the election of any but sub-
stantial Tories ; therefore, the electors must
needs send back a new crowd which would
bo just as proof against popular votes as the
old one.
# « »
Moreover, the Referendum can only affirm
principles; the details must be left, in a
great measure, to the Legislature. Where
the Legislature is elected by the people,
and is answerable to the people, it may
generally be assumed that it will endeavour
to execute the popular mandate according
to the spirit in which it is given. Where it
isn’t elected by the people, and is so pro-
tected by its property qualification that it is
scarcely answerable to anybody, and where
it is distinctly hostile to the public sent!
ment, then, if it executes the mandate at all,
it will do it entirely in its own way. In
other words, when the people have affirmed
some progressive principle, and the Assembly
has put it into form, the Council, even if it
doesn’t wholly reject it, will “amend” it
out of all semblance of the original intention.
In England, some years ago, the people
affirmed as definitely as circumstances
would admit—the principle that an employer
should he liable for injuries received by a
workman through defective machinery or
other circumstances for which the employer
was distinctly liable. The Commons passed
the measure with enthusiasm. The Lords also
passed it with enthusiasm, but with a proviso
that a workman might relieve his employer
of his liability by agreement. As the average
employer would not engage any workman
who did not relieve him of his liability by
agreement this practically nullified the prin-
ciple involved. Even if the Victorian
Council should enact the Referendum in the
first place, and should not absolutely cast
the expression of the popular will into the
waste-paper basket in the second, it would
probably carry out the national mandate in
a very similar fashion.
# * *
The Victorian Constitution is practically a
contrivance for bringing about incurable
deadlocks, and its existence is a standing
evidence of the incapacity of the British
Parliament as a constitution-maker. In
other countries various devices exist for
arriving at some definite result in cases of
disagreement. Sometimes one House is re-
cognised as the preponderating influence,
and when it has passed a hill a certain
number of times, or by a certain fixed
majority, it becomes law despite the veto of
the other Chamber. A still better and
more effective proviso, and one which
exists in many democratic countries, enacts
that in cases of disagreement the two
Chambers meet as one body, and the vote of
the majority carries the day. In Great
Britain, Queensland, Maoriland, and New
South Wales, a certain clumsy and circuitous
remedy is provided whereby the Ministry
may, after a bitter conflict with the Sovereign
or her representative, appoint a hatch of
nominees of its own way of thinking to turn
its minority into a majority. Victoria, how-
ever, has absolutely no remedy at all. By a
wonderful effort of perverted ingenuity the
Imperial Parliament supplied it with one
House which must, in the nature of things,
become more and more Democratic, and
another which must necessarily remain
bitterly Tory. Then it gave one unlimited
power of action and the other unlimited
power of obstruction, and bade them fight
the matter out to the bitter end after care-
fully providing that neither combatant could
get at the other. It is another version of
the old problem, how to add together a
mile-and-a-half and eighteen-pence, and give
the answer in cwts.
* * «
When the Victorian people elected the
present Assembly almost every candidate
was called upon to declare in favor of the
Land-tax principle, and the popular decision
on that point was as definite as a cumber-
some system would admit. Yet the nation,
by that declaration, only shook an impotent
fist at the Council, and that body took no
manner of notice. If the nation reaffirmed
the same principle by a Referendum vote it
would merely shake the same impotent fist
a little more definitely but with no more
assurance • f any good result. The llefereti
lum, in such a case, is only a means whereby
'fie people may speak a little more clearly to
iho political deaf adder which refuses to
listen, (’lie only way out of the eternal
deadlock lies in a reform of the present
hopeless Constitution, and there are only
two methods by which that reform can he.
arrived at. One is by inducing the Imperial
Parliament, which made the present, system,
to unmake it. Berry tried this device and
failed, hut, the British Cabinet of to-day is
very unlike that of Berry’s time, and the
experiment might be renewed with more
success. On the other hand, George
Turner is very unlike Berry, in the days
when that politician was still the Tribune of
Hie People, and he looks altogether unlike
the man to attempt so dangerous and unusual
an expedient. The other possibility lies in
forcing the Council to reform itself by some-
thing that—to produce such a result—must
needs look very like popular insurrection.
Either way it is necessary to decide in
advance exactly what measure of reform is
required, and it is necessary to gain as much
as possible while seeming to ask for as little as
circumstances will allow,
* • *
It may be safely assumed, to begin with,
that even an allegedly Liberal British Parlia-
ment would not enact the total abolition of
the Council, and that that body could not be
induced to totally abolish itself without an
actual—not merely au apparent—revolution.
Moreover, there is no necessity that the
Council should be abolished at present ; all
that is wanted is that it should be made
directly responsible to the people, and the
abolition would follow when required. The
desired result might be arrived at by enact-
ing that, in the case of a deadlock, the two
Houses should sic and vote as one body ;
this device is far from perfect, but it is pro-
bably the best one immediately available,
and it would, in most cases of really serious
importance, enable the popular chamber to
have its way. Another method would be to
abolish the property qualification for
M’s.L.C., to remunerate them at the same
rate as members of the Lower House, and
to make both Chambers dissoluble simul-
taneously. The Toryism of the Upper
House arises not so much because its 160,000
constituents are Conservative by instinct,
as because the property qualification
makes it impossible for any save mem-
bers of the most hopelessly Tory class
in the community to become eligible for mem-
bership, therefore the proposed change would
gradually democratise the hitherto fossilised
gathering. Either way, it is almost certain
that the Upper House would be brought so
far under popular control, as to enable the
people to reform it further without resorting
to any extraordinary methods. Also, either
way, the nation only asks as much as is
urgently necessary, and yet, while preserving
strict moderation, asks as much as is imme-
diately required. The Referendum, though
a good'and thoroughly Democratic measure,
is insufficient for the occasion. There is
nothing to be gained by enabling the people
to accurately express their policy to a Cham-
ber which isn’t responsible to the people,
and which is not required to care for any
policy save its own.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Public Grog Department.
A Sydney paper puts the case for the private
publican and against the State Drink Monopoly
as follows :
The main argument used was that under State manage-
ment there would be less drunkenness, because of the
better quality of liquor sold, and the cleanlier conditions
under which the business would be carried on. This,
however, is merely a matter of opinion. It is no more use
blaming the fusel oil or the turpentine than blaming
“the salmon,” for when all is said and done, no matter
what may be taken along with it, the main cause of
drunkenness is alcohol. It is not because the drink is
adulterated that it intoxicates, but because of the exces-
sive amount consumed. Men who drink bad spirit to
excess would be just as likely to over indulge in good
spirit, if it was available, so that, as far as temperance is
concerned, that argument does not count for much. But
if it did, there is no guarantee that we should get better
liquor in a State-conducted hotel than in a State-licensed
one. If drink is adulterated now it is done so in defiance
of the law, and there would be just the same chance of
making profit in this way by the State barman as by the
licensed publican. It is said that in Sweden the Gothen-
burg system works satisfactorily, but Sweden is a long
way off, and, besides, what might suit the Scandinavian
might not suit the Australian. As a general rule, the less
the Slate has to do in the way of buying and selling
amongst the people the better it comes off.
This statement is worth replying to, principally
because it puts the whole case for the private
fusel-oil industry into the smallest possible
compass. It isn’t much of a case, anyhow, but
it is about all there is. So far as concerns the
first part of it, alcohol is the main cause of
drunkenness ; but bad alcohol, according to any
number of seemingly reliable authorities, causes
a more violent form of drunkenness than does
the pure article. Even the journalistic drink-
apologist admits that alcohol is only the main
cause, not the sole cause; therefore, the other
cause being removed, there would, by its own
admission, be less drunkenness—how much less
is a matter of opinion. But, in addition to this,
there would be less drunkenness because there
would be far fewer drinking-shops, and because
there would be no temptation to sell drink to
people who were already intoxicated, as the
bar-keeper wouldn't trade for his own profit.
As for the statement, “there is no guarantee
that we should get better liquor in a .State-
conducted hotel than in a State-licensed one,”
it is mere drivel, and tolerably abject drivel at
that. The private hotel-keeper, when he sells
fiendish German brandy worth Is. a gallon, or
raw potato spirit illicitly manufactured in the
bush, at the price of good liquor, gets the profit
Jur himself, therefore he sells it. The civil ser-
vant who ran the State-owned pub. wouldn’t get
the profit for himself, therefore he wouldn’t sell
it. Or, to be more strictly accurate, he could only
get the profit for himself with the comparative
certainty of, sooner or later, forfeiting his Civil
Service billet, his salary and his prospective
pension, and getting several years in gaol for
embezzlement. Therefore, very few of him
would do it, and it could never become a con-
tinuous or widely-extended practice. As for
the statement that “ the less the State has to
do in the way of buying and selling, the better
it comes off,” that is only an irresponsible hat-
man opinion, backed by no kind of evidence,
and may, therefore, be passed by. If the State
is less honest than the private monopolist it
way be true ; if the State is more honest it cer-
tainly isn’t true ; if the two are only equally
honest again it isn’t true. By itself, and
unsupported by any semblance of proof,
it is just as convincing as an anonymous
denunciation on a post-card. Even a journal
with four times the circulation of any other
daily in Australia (and almost all Australian
dailies have four times the circulation of any
other daily) only speaks with the voice of one
man, and when it fails to quote a single in-
stance in support of its assertions then it is
just as reliable as the bald statement of any
other man, and no more so.
The Great Australian Wine Industry.
Mr. A. Despeissis, Consulting Viticulturist to
the N.S W. 1 >epa,rtment of Agriculture, ridicules
the idea that, the chief defects of Australian
wine are due to the “ youth ” of the beverage,
and its want of maturity He scouts the verity
of the very frequent remark—“ The wine is all
right; all it wants is age.” He tells Australian
vignerons bluntly that they don’t know their
business, from the choosing of the varieties of
grape to the fermenting and “ rearing ” of the
wine. He admits that age accentuates the
defects, so that the trend of his argument
would seem to be, not only that most Australian
wine is not all right except for age, but that,
though it may not be bad now, it will grow bad
with age ; and, if bad now, it will get worse
with age. “In N.S.W. wine-cellars are thou-
sands of gallons of very promising young wines
from last year’s vintage. In two years’ time
they will be found utterly tainted and ruined
through no fault of their own.” Mr Des-
peissis calls for a comprehensive Vine and
Fruit Pest Act, the establishment of a College
of Viticulture, possessing a vineyard, a well
organised cellar, and an oeuological labora-
tory, where analyses would be math ; also,
a strictly enforced Food Adulteration Act
and a revised Distillation Act. With tome
very keen observations on the iniquities of the
present New South Wales Distillation Act, the
premium it offers to “ mock brandy and patent
whisky,” and the evils of lunacy that follow in
their train, Mr. Bespeissis pictured the possi-
bilities of a market for a good, wholesome,
home-made brandy. The gist of his sermon
was that Victoria and South Australia succied
belter than New South Wales, because in tie
two first-named provinces nearly every district
has a central winery that turns the produce of
Ihe vineyards into wine according to approved
and scientific methods, whereas in New South
Wales each small grape-grower is his own wine-
maker, and is a wine-maker according to his
own loose and imperfect notions, and has no
properly-equipped cellars. The way out for
New Soutli Wales vignerons is, by organisation
and co.-operation, to establish central wineries
and a central wine association.
White v. Yellow.
“ The white man with the yellow money versus
the yellow man with the white money,” formed
the text of the most eloquent part of Morton
Frewen’s lecture on the “ Prevailing Industrial
Depression” in Melbourne last week. This
apostle of silver has been celebrated in Stead’s
paltering novel, “Our Splendid Paupers.”
Morton Frewen is a travelled man, and a man
of wide sympathies, and he probably realised
the humor of his surroundings at Melbourne
Athenaeum. The official bimetallists of Victoria
consist, for the most part, of the “ Young Tory
Patriotic League” and the “Tariff Reform
League,” and the several other disguises in
which the bank-clerks, and shipping-clerks,
and wool-clerks down South delight to disguise
themselves. Morton Frewen, who believes
himself to be fighting in the democratic cause,
had Softgoods Sargood for chairman, and
Budget Downes Carter to second the vote of
thanks, and brewer Fitzgerald, M.L.C., to
vocally support Sargood. When the lecturer
referred to “the pretty platitudes of the
Cobdbn Club, ’ it was probably for the pleasure
of seeing a row of Tories squirm in public,
which they did. Morton Frewen unfolded a
gospel of bimetallism abundantly more coherent
and reasonable than the Victorian Bimetallic
League ever reckoned upon. He gave his ad-
herence to the charge that the demonetisation
of silver was a deliberate crime, com-
mitted in the cause of usury, and that
England was an accomplice after the
fact. He is logical enough to admit chat
if the bi-metallic tie was effectively main-
tained by France singlehanded, then the practi-
cal adherence of any “first-class nation” to
bi-metallism would be sufficient to restore
silver. These are aspects of the question not
heard locally befoie. But “ Rawton Silver-
tongue,” as Stead calls him, proves too much
when he proves that Australian railways lau-


guish by reason of the fall in rupee exchange.
Australian railways, as administered by
Sfeioht, and Australian railways, as adminis-
tered by Eddy, come not in the same category.
Bi-metallists prove too much when they prove
against mono-metallism all the evils that Aus-
tralia lias suffered by reason of boom-madness
and boom-robbery. And it may be doubted
whether the racial issue involved in the “ white
man with the yellow money vermis the yellow
man with the white money ” will ever be settled
on the exchanges. The yellow man in Australia
is not tamed by mono-metallism. Whether he
is paid in terms of gold or of silver he can and
does underbid white labor. In Australia, with
many legislative restrictions, the yellow man is
a perpetual menace—and it is therefore only
natural that the advent of Asia as a competitor
in the world’s markets should materially incom-
mode. “ civilised ” industry. It is hard to see
how a fixed ratio between gold and silver would
seriously affect this racial issue. In the Dis-
pensation of Supply and Demand, the yellow-
man, who has about as few wants as a pig,
must have the advantage of cheapness. The
white-man must go. But, in communities
where the principle of Protection is upheld, the
white-man does not go—the yellow-man has to
stay away. At present Australia is suffering
from a complication of diseases, everyone of
which can be proved :
(1) Government by Contradiction and undemocratic
modes of electing legislators.
(2) Maladministration of railways and public works.
(3) Unscientific taxation, the interest on borrowings
spent on the land not being obtained therefrom, hut from
people who never benefited by the borrowings.
(4) Bank fiascoes, the nightmares of finance, caused
partly by noodledom and partly by knavery.
(5) Delayed Federation.
Morton Frewen, with marvellous statistical
lucidity, invites Australia to put a finger in
the bimetallic pie, but the pie into which Aus-
tralian affairs have been knocked claims first
attention, and probably when she has set her-
self up again Australia will choose to meddle
not at all with outside affairs.
Combination v. Competition.
During the trial in connection with the recent
tobacco robberies in Melb. the following evi-
dence concerning an importers’ ring was given :
Edward Stevens, manager for Henry Berry, examined
by Detective-Sergeant Sainsbury, said the m ichanls were
bound not to sell Victory, Golden Eagle, or Williams’s
aromatic tobacco under Is. lid. per lb. in caddies of 201 b.
each, and the duty was 3s. per lb. The price was, there-
fore, practically ss. per lb. wholesale.
Mr. Coldiia.m : This seems to he the outcome of a
“ring,” isn’t it ?
Witness: The whole case is the outcome of a “ ring.”
(Laughter).
Wasn’t there a “ring” to keep up the price of tobacco?
—All merchants obtain the tobacco at the same price from
Couchb, Calder and Co., the sole importers. There was
an agreement that all should sell at the same price. We
are under a £5O penalty not to sell at less.
Have you heard of people se.ling under cost price?—
Yes, but not these tobaccos.
Isn’t there really a “trades-union trust” or ring to
keep up the price?—No, it is simply to ensure a profit to
a!l concerned.
Nemesis ordained that this further evidence ot
importers’ tricks should be elicited by the
barrister who contested St. Kilda in the Free-
trade interest last elections. Needless to say,
the Tory press did not break out next morning
in leaded invectives against the “ ring ’ and its
resistance to the eternal laws of supply and
demand. Experience, indeed, has shown that
such combinations in the present state of affairs
can not only be formulated, but can be main-
tained successfully—and, perhaps, this is why
the Tory press did not attack the Tory im-
porters in the matter. Day by day the pre-
cepts and practices of the Tories grow
wider apart. The precepts get duller and
the practices become sharper. Tory bankers and
Tory importers set up politicians and editors to
proclaim doctrines which it is the daily task of
the said bankers and importers to disprove.
The price of everything such men deal in, from
bank-interest to bicycles, is fixed by combina-
tion and maintained by a “ ring and the day
is past for them, as well as for Democracy,
when the vague sophistries of the Manchester
School have serious attention in daily life. In
fact, the bogey “ laws ” of the Tory party are
only trotted out when there is agitation for
combination on behalf of the general com-
munity. It is then that the eternal
verities of political bosh find loudest ex-
position, and for the fortieth time the
Tory rally is promised. In the meantime, the
importing rings go on prosperously “ ensuring
a profit to all concerned.” And there is no
reason why a fair profit should not be ensured
to all concerned—the trouble is that secret
combinations generally aim at an unfair profit—
and the public is robbed right and left. It
might do some good to compel registration,
under a heavy penalty for non-compliance,
of the charters of all “trusts,” “combina-
tions,” and “ rings.” The public need not fear
the open foe—it is the secret foe that cannot
he guarded against.
A Land-Grab Case.
An appeal case, which is now dragging its pro-
tracted length along before the Frivy Council
at enormous cost to the N. S. Wales Treasury,
is remarkable as one of the finest examples on
record of the gall of the private landowner. A
good many years ago the local “Crown”
granted to somebody or other—The Bulletin
either forgets or never knew the name of the
grantee—a large area of land at Cremorae, on
Sydney harbour. In the deed the said Crown
expressly reserved to itself a strip along the
shore 100 feet wide, measuring from high-water
mark. The grantee’s land started from the
further side of this strip, and, according to the
explicit terms of the deed, he had no more claim
to the portion reserved than he had to Bucking-
ham Palace, or the delta of the Nile. Never-
theless, when, in 1888 or 1889, the grant was
eut up for sale by auction, the reserved
portion was peacefully included in the area
for sale. To prevent all misconception, the
mictioneer impressively read out that clause
m the deed which specified that the owner had
>m earthly right to the water-frontage, and
then explained that “we” utterly refused to
recognise the reservation, and were going to
sell the reserved portion at the buyer’s risk.
The offer excited no enthusiasm, and the water-
frontage remained unsold. After that litigation
commenced, and the case was promptly decided
in favor of the State with damages. Then in
1891, the grantees applied to the Full Court for
a new trial, which was emphatically refused.
Next, the case was dragged to the Privy
Council, which is reported as having
“reserved its decision.” What the decision
will be when it ceases to be reserved no one
can tell at present, but as to the bill of costs
there can be no possible shadow of doubt
whatever. The case for the grantees rests
on 00 years’ alleged possession, and the case
for the Crown depends to some extent on a
letter written in 1878 asking that the grant
should be issued, which the Full Court held
as proof that the alleged possession—if there
was any, and if adverse possession counts
against the Crown, which, according to common
opinion, it does not—is now Only 1(5 years old
at the most. But, apart from these legal quib-
bles, the wild humor of the situation lies in the
fact that the State may sell a certain well-
defined piece of land at a low figure, and then
have to spend the purchase-money several times
over in law-courts to prevent the buyer tak-
ing the State’s own adjacent property, when he
doesn’t even pretend to show that he paid for
it or has any documentary title to it. The law
of the plaintifi’s case may be all right, for all
The Bulletin knows to the contrary, but its
moral aspect looks atrocious, and it seems about
time that all titles by possession were formally
abolished by Act of Parliament. Where a
landowner disappears leaving his property un-
claimed it should revert, not to anybody who
likes to take possession of it, but (as in Maori-
land) to the State. The title by possession,
whether in the case of private or Crown lands,
is a legalised steal pure and simple.
Unprotection.
An old allegory has it that a farmer put cats in
the cheese-room to prevent the ravages of rats,
and the cats ate the cheese. The Vic. Tariff
Board has just elicited the allegory in this
shape :
Mr. J. F. Richardson, manager of the Colonial Sugar
Refinery Company, was called. He was asked to give his
reasons why the differential rate, involving concession to
the local industry of £1 per ton, should not be abolished.
What is the product of the local refinery ?—34,790 tons
per annum.
Then your company has been making £1 profit on
each ton to the amount of close on £35,000 ?—No
answer.
The loss to the Customs revenue since 1887 has been
over £200,000. What was your dividend last year?—Ten
per cent.
How much did you carry forward ? About £7OOO. The
dividend absorbed £82,000 of the profits.
Has your company any differential rate in the other
colonies ?—No.
Yet you refine successfully there?—Yes.
Is it true that you control the market of Victoria?—
No ; not altogether.
Is it true, then, that you are gelling your sugar in New
South Wales at a lower price than in Victoria ? Prices
are about the same.
Now, why should your company extract £35,000 per
annum out of the revenue of this overburdened colony
when you are able to pay such enormous profits ? —We
could do witli less.
Do you not undersell any shipment that arrives, so as
to keep the business to yourself ?—Yes. We reduce our
prices.
Is it true you can wipe out all competition from this
market?—We never do it.
Does your company require the differential rate any
longer?—Yes. If we had not some encouragement we
would manufacture in Sydney and Brisbane, where we
could get coal cheaper, and where it would suit us better
in many ways. We could manufacture at seven shillings
cheaper in New South Wales.
The Freetrader would advocate the abolition of
the protective cats; but the Protectionist would
simply keep the cats for their purpose, and
prevent them from getting more than a reason-
able portion of the cheese. In the case in point
the Colonial Sugar Refinery, which lately ab-
sorbed its nominal opponent, has in form and
fact become an aggressive monopoly. It has
never served a true protective purpose. The
Protection that would play off Sydney against
Melbourne, or Melbourne against Brisbane, is
mere parochial inanity. Each city has its place
in Australian unity, and the sooner Australian
imposts are taken off Australian sugars the
better.
Some Victims of Competition.
The Vic. Tariff Enquiry Board is largely a trap
for woolly - headed Freetraders, who came
snorting into the board-room armed with argu-
ments against their blessed creed. The louder
a witness cries for lower duties the more reason
he shows, in almost every case, for a further
10 per cent, addition to his burden of woe.
Having hoisted himself with his own petard he
hugs the strange delusion that he has blown
Protection to bits, and awaits with confidence
an Argus account of how he “ emphatically
condemned the tariff.” The virtues of Protec-
tion can be made more clear by an indignant
importer than by any Trades Hall orator on
earth, judging from the latest Melbourne
revelations. For instance, David Altson,
manufacturer and importer of saddlery and
harness, informed the Enquiry Board that
the high tariff had ruined him. When
the duties were moderate he did a
good trade, but now he doesn’t. He ascribed
tliis personal calamity to bad times and “ ex-
cessive coin-petition, caused by the high tariff.'”
Duly 50 saddle-trees were imported into Vic-
toria last year, and he was the man who im-
ported them. “As soon as a hoy can make a
saddle he starts as a manufacturer. So long
as it is a saddle of some sort people will
buy it,” he said. Thus the trade slips
away from middleman Altson, and that’s
where his ruination comes in. E. Evans, an
ex-portmanteau-maker, was another witness
with a Freetrade grievance. “He had to give
up business as a manufacturer on account of the
competition induced by the tariff.” When the
duty was only 10 per cent, he could manu-
facture at a profit, but when the higher duty
quite blocked the way for foreign portmanteaux,
smaller manufacturers came into the trade and
knocked F. Evans out of time. The style of
;>■eduction, by the way, must have mightily
.i proved whilst Evans was losing his business,
seeing that “no duty is required now, for the
local article is cheaper than the imported.”
Yet Evans thinks that lower duties would
give “ a healthier tone ” to the business.
Altson and Evans are two fair specimens of
the Freetrade, alias Tariff Amendment party
in Victoria. They object to Protection because
it bas made manufacturers of “ boys’’—who
ought to be filling humble positions in the
employ of Altson or Evans. Saddles and
portmanteaux of local production supply all, or
nearly all, the local demand, but there is no
satisfaction in that circumstance for men who
ceased to flourish when Victoria left off send-
ing money abroad for these goods. Where is
the good of Protection, if it induces keen com-
petition, and spoils the fine, old-fashioned,
gigantic-profit businesses of Altson and
Evans ?
Protected Hats.
The following extraordinary statement about
the cost of hats in England and Victoria is
taken from the evidence of an importer (the
agent of Christie and Co., London) before Vic.
Tariff Board ;
The lowest-priced hats were made in Victoria for 7s. 6d.
per dozen, while in England they cost 145., which with
duty and charges amounted to 20s. a dozen landed in
Victoria. This large difference left ample room for a
reduction of duty. Hard felt hats were made in Victoria
for tos. a dozen, and in England for 16s. a dozen. Duty
and charges brought the price of English hard felt hats
landed in Victoria up to (17s. per dozen. On men’s hats,
which cost 20s. per dozen to manufacture in Victoria and
30s. in England, there was also great room for reductions.
This is the Argus report, and the items have
been compared with the Age report and found
to agree exactly. The importing class usually
declares that manufacturing is necessarily dearer
in Australia than England ; but when it has to
turn the opposite plea into an argument for
Freetrade, it probably would not hesitate to
stultify itself by a right-about-face. In this
case the importer may be telling the truth,
though it seems almost too good to be true.
The importer pleads for a reduction in the
tariff to enable him to rehabilitate his vanished
trade. Of course, the Vic. market would not
be immediately rushed, and efforts made at any
expense to crush out the local manufacturer in
the interests of the importer. No ; The
Bulletin believes that the time has now come
in Vic. when the State should assist the im-
porters who have been led astray by Freetrade
fallacies. The poor wretches have not only had
their pet theories turned inside-out by Protec-
tion, bub they have lost their living as well.
The time is now come for the abolition of the
protective tariff on hats, and the institution in
the interest of the importers of an excise-duty
on locally-made hats, so that the product of
England, which country has not the manufac-
turing advantages of Victoria, may compete on
equal terms with the Vic. product within Vic.
boundaries.
It should be the early business of some Demo-
cratic and patriotic Australian member of Par-
liament in each Assembly of the Australasian
provinces to submit to the House of which he is
a member some such resolutions as these
(1) That this House declares that it is not only unneces-
sary hut prejudicial to Australian interests that any Aus-
tralian Judge should be appointed a member of the
Judicial Committee of the British Privy Council; and
that this House absolutely refuses to vote any portion of
the salary of any such Privy Councillor.
(2.) That the time has arrived for the establishment of
an Australian Federal Court of Appeal for the final hear-
ing and settlement of all purely Australian legal disputes.
(a) That it be an instruction to the Government to
take immediate steps, with the other Australian Govern-
ments, for the establishment of an Australian Federal
Court of Appeal.
(4.) That the Governor be requested to forward these
resolutions to the British authorities.
Let one People’s Member in each People’s House
move some such resolutions as the above, and
let the people watch and note which represen-
tatives are faithful to their popular trust, and
which are ready to aid in the consummation of
the biggest lawyers’ job ever yet proposed in
Australia. The submission of direct resolutions
is the readiest way of completely checkmating
the audacious thimble-rigging of the so-called
Federal Council.
There is a temptation to hail the Tory journa-
list as the humorist of all centuries. The poli-
tical leading articles of the S.M. Herald and
the Argus, for instance, often leave the idea
that they are written by a coterie of practical
jokers, whose greed for laughter is so excessive
that nothing short of the daily befoolment of a
nation will satisfy them. In a recent issue of
the Argus a practical joke on Humor itself is
attempted. In a review of a book by one
Agnes Repplier (no woman, by the way, was
ever much of a humorist) the Argus says :
Mr. Mark Twain’s “ A Yankee at the Court of King
Arthur” was immensely admired in America, while in
England it is considered a piece of profane buffoonery,
equally/ vulgar and pointless. It is much to Miss
Repplier s credit that she says nothing of Mark Twain.
As a set-off to this, a parson named Hole is
held' up to admiration.
His chapter on “Bores” is especially delightful for its
wit, gaiety, and a pleasant exaggeration in*the style of
Sydney Smith. Nearly every sentence embodies some
wholesome jest.- There is the story of the deaf man who
meeting a friend of the bore tribe, produced carefully
from his pocket an elaborate ear-trumpet, one end of
which having been adjusted with some pains in the
defective organ the other was handed with a smile to the
bore’s mouth, who shouted down it, “ You are breaking
very last.’’
The italics are supplied by the Argus. It
remains to be seen whether the S.M. Herald
will be able to do anything to equal the Argus’
practical joke, Hole v. Mark Twain. It is
possible that the S.M. Herald won’t need the
help of the italics. We shall see.
Some enlightened and enterprising statesman
who wants to reduce the outgoings of Australia
might turn his attention with advantage to the
patent-medicine question. This country, on a
rough estimate, sends away annually £250,000
for patent-medicines alone. A very large pro-
portion of these is admittedly rubbish of the
meanest descriptions, and a great part of the
balance may be declared with confidence to be
of very little account. No intelligent person
takes patent medicines seriously, or is ever
likely to do so. They are the fetish of the
ignorant and superstitious who love to revel in
pills and in great quantities of loathsome
draughts and potions, and who put an evil-
smelling enemy into their mouths to steal away
their bowels. The average patent-medicine sells
for about nine times its intrinsic cost, even
estimating that cost on the liberal scale
of the apothecary, and it fetches that
exorbitant price solely because each P.nmnmi rv /I
has its own particular crowd of worshippers
who—having come to believe that they live by
it alone —would probably buy it at three times
the price if necessary. Considering the amount
of good legal tender which is sent abroad
annually on account of this superstition, and
how little value is obtained in return, some
enterprising Covernment might make a start by
declaring that no patent for any medicine, and
no copyright of the name of any proprietary
remedy, shall hold good within its province.
Another way of meeting the case would be to
levy 1000 per cent, duty on all imported
patent medicines, and still another would be to
prohibit their importation altogether. Just
now Australia wants to cut down its outgoings
as far as possible, and, therefore, The Bulle-
tin looks with an unfriendly eye on that
<£250,000 per annum which is sent abroad to
buy the means of wrecking this nation’s
digestion, and enamelling its bright young
countenance, and vainly smearing the bald spot
on its youthful head, and otherwise attempting
to lend it a fatal beauty which is not its own.
A certain Miss Lee, acting as spokesfemale of
a goody-goody society at Wellington (M.L.),
demands that “marriages should be strictly
supervised by the State, and that no unhealthy
couples, or women under 20 or over 40 years of
age, or men under 25 or over 45 years of age,
should be allowed to marry.” The idea—espe-
cially as concerns the “unhealthy couples is
theoretically perfect, but The Bulletin doubts
much if Miss Lee has the courage to effectually
carry out her theory. It has met many
gaunt and enthusiastic females with a similar
theory, but it never yet met one with sufficient
pluck to carry it to its necessary and logical
conclusion. The trouble lies, not in the mar-
riage of old and unhealthy persons, which
really doesn’t matter, hut in their having
children and thus handing down their
shattered and diseased constitutions to future
generations. If they can be prevented
from marrying and handing down the
shattered constitution lawfully, they will do
it unlawfully. Therefore, the well-meaning
society brings up with a crash against the
following series of alternatives .-—(I) All
girls under 20 and unmarried women over 40
must be kept in strict confinement. (2) All
youths under 25 must be kept in strict confine-
ment. (3) All men. over 45 must either
be executed, or imprisoned for life, or
rendered incapable of propagating their
species. Or else (4) all children, legitimate or
otherwise, born of unhealthy parents, or of
parents of prohibited ages, must be killed ; and
(5) all illegitimate children whose fathers are
unknown must he killed in any case, on the off-
chance that the missing parent is over age,
or under age, or disqualified by ill-health.
And, speaking of ages, what age is Miss Lee ?
Also, what is the average age of the gooefy-
goody society which she represents? It «mst
have quite a lot of creases in its neck.
A veteran N.S.W. fruitgrower told the
Sydney conference last week that, if canned Aus-
tralian fruits only had a Californian label on the
can, the consumers were willing to pay an extra
6d. a tin for them. Just so. Australia is its own
INazareth in all things.

By the introduction of a new machine for
oiling locomotives, which exactly regulates the
supply according to the requirements, instead of
‘sloshing ” the lubricator on by hand in the old
style, the annual oil-bill on the Vic. railways has
been reduced from ,£27,000 to about.£lo,ooo.
Dear Bulletin, —In a recent issue you
suggested that author Anthony Hope might be
related to Vic.’s Gov. This is not so. Anthony
Hope Hawkins is the son of liev. William Haw-
king vicar of St. Bride’s, in the city of Loudon.
He was president of the Oxford Union once, is an
ardent Badical and a barrister, though latterly he
has thrown over law for books. His “Half a
Hero,” dealing with Australian gubernatorial life
led many wise critics to assume an Australian
birthright for him. As a matter of fact, he has
never been to this country. —Yours, R.C.B.
London Literary World—a dull little
weekly which makes a more or less dishonest
living by scissoring long passages from newly-
published books and hashing them up with a few
lines of inept comment—and which wouldn’t be
here noticed but that it has been quoted with
sweet approval by certain Australian friends of
The Bulletin— naturally appreciates the talents
displayed by one E. H. Canuey in the clipping
without acknowledgment, from various Australian
sources (including The Bulletin) enough litera-
ture to liven-up a volume called “ The Land of the
Dawning ” (stolen title). These birds of a feather
unite in hatred of The Bulletin, and the
Literary World remarks that this paper is,
“ without question, the vulgarest in the English
language ; it is the organ of the Australian larri-
kin (stolen phrase). Such testimonials as these
from the galled jades of cant, rant and wooden-
headed “respectability ” are inexpressibly cheer-
ing to The Bulletin aforesaid.

Geo. Rignold’s pantomime, “The House
that Jack Built,” will close at the end of this
week after a run of two months. Business on the
whole has been profitable, especially considering
that the public enthusiasm about pantomime isn’t
what ft was. On Saturday “ Black-eyed Susan ”
is to be staged, with a burlesque to follow. Her
Majesty’s, by the way, now hangs out the baleful
announcement that the free-list has been suspended
by the neck until it is dead, dead, dead and utterly
deceased.
* * *
The “ Union Jack” is to be staged next
Saturday at Sydney Royal, and some spirit-
stirring allusions to the manner in which the old
flag waves, and the dangers which arise when you
tread on its tail and thus arouse its heart of oak,
may be confidently looked for. Bland Holt’s
season, so far, has been the best thing that has
happened to the Royal for a long while. Even
Maggiie Moore, despite her success, did not wake
up that old establishment so effectively as Bland
has done of late.
** * *
To-morrow (Friday), Titheradge sows the
wind at Sydney Lyceum for the last time, and
thereafter the moral that is contained in his gouty
foot will be seen no more during the present
season. At this late hour, it has just occurred to
The Bullet.n that the ancient text needs to be
revised and brought' up to date; nowadays, he
who sows the wind reaps, not the whirlwind, bub
an enormously swollen hoof, which ripens in his
latter years, and stays with him like a bulbous
ghost of the drinks that he had in his youth.
Titheradge’s gout is a work of art, and suggests
long study of the misshapen extremities of many
superannuated and corny revellers. Also, it
arouses the reflection that the sere-and-yellow
tvvell generally repents with his feet, when he
t-pents at all. On Saturday, the B. and B. co.
makes a new departure with the “Sportsman”
(produced by arrangement with Plain Bill Hol-
loway) and the “ Open Gate,” a one-act play by
Haddon Chambers, both of which pieces have
never yet been seen in Sydney. The new bill is
to boom for six nights only.
* iff * *
When last heard from, Woodloch’s circus
and water-pantomime were still in evidence in the
vicinity of Redfern railway-station, to their own
satisfaction and that of the community at large.
* * * -»
Although Melbourne didn’t ask for a third
pantomime in one season the “ Cinderella” experi-
ment, at the Princess’s, is quite successful. Miss
Ida Osborne is not much like the ideal younger
sister that Nellie Stewart created in days of yore,
but she sings prettily and plays her little part
with spriglitlinesa. The silvery tenor is not
allowed to leave the stage until he has accepted a
second encore and told his love for Sally,
and, notwithstanding the absurdity of that
mawkish ballad, he gets much applause
for the effort. No man, of course, could
live in an alley in patent-leather boots. The
Baron’s delicately-modulated foot-notes inspire
sympathy even in the dress-circle, where a clog-
dancer seldom finds friends, also the ballets and
the general gorgeousness of “ Cinderella ” are
matters for admiring comment. The theatre
itself, as already mentioned, is a feast of new
beauty. Anyone who doesn’t trouble to contem-
plate the decorations can stroll out into the vesti-
bule and sniff the odour of paint drying away in
the distance.
*■ * *• *
Harry Rickards awoke Melbourne Opera
House on Saturday with a vigor worthy of that
indomitable warrior. A fine large waterfall at
the rear gave an air of distance and distinction to
the opening “set,” and a new variety company,
mostly taken over from Sydney Tivoli, made
things fairly lively, apart from the sole lessee.
When Mr. H. Rickards came up with his “little
lot,” the festivity w.axed furious. In a few
minutes he had fully 50 per cent, of a crowded
audience singing the martial chorus of “ ’E dunno
where ’e are,” and the mfection appeared to be
spreading so fast that there wouldn’t have been a
silent member left in the theatre if the chorus had
held out' a_ little longer. They would all have
assisted to swell the triumph of ’Arry’s strain.
Finally, he made a speech full of emotion and art-
ful business announcements, from which his
auditors, who by this time loved Rickards better
than life itself, gathered that he was game to act
handsomely by them on fair and reasonable provo-
cation. Hhe found it good enough to run the
dingy old houses he-would be good enough to put
a new face on it entirely, said the youthful
veteran.
** * *
The ©acres a»e understood, at time of
writing, to be due at Melbourne Bi|ou on Satur-
day, when they will produce “ A Wife’s Ordeal,”
for the first time, worth mentioning, on any stage.
It is a melodramatic play, of the purely domestic
ordfef, designed by G. R. Sims for the two artists,
who bear most of the heat and burden of
the plot. Miss Amy Roselle has to pull through
the ordeal aforesaid, whilst her husband ap-
pears as a highly-polished villain. Elton
takes a short, but sweet, comedy part.
Miss Mabel Hardy, from Great Britain, and
Wilfrid Shine are in the cast ; and the artless
young girl of the story is Mias Muriel Carr, whose
portrait embellishes The Bulletin’s latest num-
ber. There are but two other minor characters in
“ A Wife’s Ordeal,” which spreads over four acts
and requires tirst-class acting, accordingly. There
is no opposition novelty to clash with the Dacres’
debut, so the Bijou ought to be crowded on Satur-
day for the first time these two years.
* * -* *
The pantomime season at the Royal closed
on Saturday last. At the matinee performance a
deputation of Chow children testified to Maggie
Moore’s kindness of heart. In the evening the
leading lady received a load of floral compliments,
and after curtain-fall, it is said, shed a few fare-
well tears. Miss Maggie Moore and Co. leave for
Adelaide this week, thence to Broken Hill and
other places before reaching Sydney.

David Syme moralises re the bloatered
Earl of Yarmouth :
Were it not for the imperious dicta of omnipotent
fashion, no one will venture to say that the earl’s high-
kiaking in the cause of shanty has anything in it that is
not entirely praiseworthy.
If charity is the gearl’s sole excuse, why not go
round with a mon-key on an organ in the sacred
cause? or stand at street corners and invite the
lord-loving people to drop shillings in his gaudy
coronet? The earl’s idiotsyncrasy might be excus-
ableuf it were clever; but the gyrations of a clumsy
noble who fancies he can dance and invites people
to pay to find out his mistake can never be praise-
worthy, In point of fact, the earl is artistically
below the level of the two-headed sheep at the
snide museum.

** * *
The vaunted Tasmanian Exhibition, at
Hobart, has settled down to be a profound and un-
obtrusive failure. Solemn silence pervades the
great, half-empty barn, and visitors go round
with an air of hushed reverence, as though on a
visit to a cathedral. They speak in whispers, and
walk on tiptoe over the rough plank floors, so as
not to wake the echoes. If they speak loudly, an
official approaches heavily but 'stealthily, and be-
seeches them in a husky undertone to be more
subdued. Lately, some side-show people came
along and filled the place with stentorian apneals
to the people to roll up. Then, for a day or'two,
the thin audience awoke, and seemed almost
cheerful, till the Cmrarnission'ers descended heavily
on the side-show desecrators, and ordered that,
for the future, they must shout in a whisper. The
said shows paid from .£25 to .£5O apiece for the
right'to exhibit, and, as no one can run a side-
show in decorous silence, the large, dank hand of
authority has practically wiped them out, and
they talk of leaving. Also, some of the Exhi-
bition shareholders think of abandoning the whole
desolate undertaking, and closing up two months
before their appointed time. The great Exhi-
bition is the flattest thing that ever happened,
even in Tasmania.

The N.S.W. Executive Commissiolier’s
report is not the only expensive job issued by the
Government Printer in connection with Chicago
Show. What has become of the tens of thousands
of copies of “The Drama and Music in N.S. W.,’’
which poor W. F. Terry (who deserves all the
credit for fixing up the N.S.W. Court) was sup-
posed to distribute, to let the Americans know
that a corrobboree, or even a circus, was not the
only entertainment in Australia? Is. 6d. is the
price demanded for the book on its cover; but
people wouldn’t take it for nothing. Terry got
quite ashamed of the thing at last, and hid the
books away under the counters that ran around
the court. When R. S. Smythe arrived at
Chicago, Terry asked him, being in the line, what
he thought he had better do with them. The
Much-Travelled advised him to burn them. No
American man or woman could possibly feel the
slightest interest in the music and drama of Aus-
tralia; the only people who might care to see the
book were old Australian pro.’s, and they were
mostly dead. What has become of those tons of
printing? Did Terry take Smythe’s advice, and
was that the eause of the fire at the World’s Fair?

Katharine Hagjy, who doddie-doddied with the (searl
of Yarmouth at JJflb. Bijou, has now shaken ofl the dust
of burlesque and Ts going to stick to comedy. She is a
pretty girl, with a natural fund of wit and more intel-
ligence than marks the usual stage aspirant. She is
chaperoned at all her stage engagements by a big sister.
The first thing a stage novice must learn is to stand up
straight. Girls seem to be worse sinners than young men
in this respect. If the average debutante held a long
piece of string in her mouth, the other end of it would
touch the ground about 6in. in front of her boots.
When Nina Boucioauit relinquished her part in the
London production of “Charley's Aunt,” it was taken up
by Mabel Lane, daughter of the high priestess of the City
Club Hotel, Melbourne. The attractive Mabel was the
daughter known as “ Chios ” behind the bar, on account
of her facial resemblance to an undraped young person in
a certain French picture. “Chloe” (the picture, not
Miss L.) is well-nigh forgotten now. A dozen years ago,
in the darker ages of Melbourne art, she hung on the wall
of the Public Gallery in Swanston-street, whilst the Scotch
Saw bath fraternity cursed her on their knees at night and
cal «d upon the Government to take her away. Finally,
Napoleonic Fitzgerald, the surgeon, bought poor Chloe
and took her away, after which the Kirk breathed more
freely. Nowadays, the dark-haired nymph could defy
Pharisaism to shift her from the Gallery. Melbourne
has been educated up to her chaste nudity in the mean-
time.

On Boxing-night, at the Elephant and Castle Theatre
(London), the clown went on and fooled with wooden legs
of mutton whilst his wife lay cold in the green-room. She
played in the opening, but had the misfortune to drop
dead at the wings after singing in a duet with another
girl. [The post-mortem showed that she died of light-
lacing.] The pantomime went on as though nothing had
happened, ditto the clown as aforesaid. This story
wouldn’t be complete without a mention of the inevitable
tears that washed the paint off the widower’s face whilst
a sympathetic brother mummer was making him up for
the part. The idi a of “the broken-hearted clown
struggling bravely against his grief ” has been worn
threadbare in fiction, but here it is in fact.
Yarmouth, on succeeding to the Hertford title, will
become the patron of three clerical livings. When he
distributes them, he ought not to forget Lauri’s Vicar of
Bray.

Luscombe Searelle took the chair at the Savage Club
gathering, one night a while ago, but the privilege cost
him dear. It seems that a subscription-list had been
opened for the widow of Gwyliym Crowe. Searelle called
attention to this deserving case, and offered as, haw 1
chairman, to put his name down for the highest amount
that any other gentleman would like to subscribe. Little
W. S. Penley promptly oast a gloom over Searelle’s soul
by passing up a cheque for £25, whereat the club chuckled
with Savage delight. The chairman had not considered
Penley’s affluence before he so rashly spoke, or perhaps
it didn’t occur to him that any actor who could afford a
£25 joke would be glad to play it on the inflated person
from South Africa.

Arthur Roberts, interviewed a few weeks ago, said he
thought he would have to do his Claude Duval as a one-
man show, so many performers being invalided. He says
actors and actresses live too fast, and their stamina is so
low that when a death comes along, like Fred. Leslie’s or
Edith Chester’s, that alone makes a lot of them ill.

Dear Bulletin,—Why don’t Australian impresarios look

bash Melba lingiially languishing (copyright) for lack of
opportunity. I have just come away from hearing a lovely
voice belonging to a young bush-girl, and wish 1 hail
money to take her South and make our fortunes. I
haven’t: and doubtless some fat-head will come along and
make the last state of that girl a replica of the barren
drudgery of her first. Kookaburra. Mareeba, N.Q.

Violet Cameron was lately singing at Collins’, a second-
rate music hall in the northern quarter of London. We

The Queen, we read, has been graciously pleased to
enquire after the health of actress Mrs. Beerbohm Tree and
her new baby. This royal solicitude is accounted for by
the recent appearance of Mrs. Tree at Balmoral, when the
company had to bolt away to Dublin a few minutes after
the curtain fell. Naturally, Her Gracious felt interested
under the circumstances.
Once upon a time an Adelaide manager who had a
grudge against the papers invited all the youngsters
(office-boys and printer’s devils) connected with the two
dailies to see his play, ushered them into a conspicuous
section of the dress-circle, put up a notice that “These
seats are reserved for members of the press,” and then
went about among the audience, and, pointing to the
notice, said, in aggrieved tones: “Those are the sort of
ignoramuses the newspapers send to criticise our per-
formance.”

A Louden paper remarks that the fun of “Dr. Bill ” is
already old-fashioned, and the once riotous “kangaroo
dance ” now seems a mild enough affair. Which doesn’t
alter the fact that the very old comedies are still the very
giddiest that a manager dare stage. Think of Maw-
worm in “The Hypocrite”—“ I was married to my wife
for many years before she blessed me with a child, but
then Dr. Cantwell came and used to pray with my wife—
alone - and now she’s got a baby I O ! he’s a good man.”
Surely the time is ripe for revivals of “ The Hypocrite ”
and its like.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who make#
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

The Mapriland Insurance Co.’s Deposit Act,
under which life assurance societies doing business in the
province have to make a deposit with the Public Trustee
as security for their local policyholders, is now in force.
The first office to arrive and plank down its £50,000 was
the Mutual Life of Australasia.

from the recent panic, and now have buyers at 20s. Com-
merdUl of Australia preference shares (£lO paid up)
which fell to 205., did a rapid recovery to 405., and last
Saturday were again slightly on the down grade. Bank
of N.Z. (£5 55.) are offered persistently at ss. with no
buyer to lie heard of at the figure. Others remain very
much as before, or thereabouts.
* * • *
It is now broadly hinted in the daily press
that the Commercial Bank of Australia won’t meet its
postponed deposits when they fall due, but w ill endeavour
to make some new arrangement with its creditors.
“As for the old bank,” said James Service, M.L.C. and
chairman of directors, “it would he able to show, at the
end of 3.j years, when its liabilities matured, such a state
of things as would entitle them to the benefit of the
principle upon which business was usually conducted
betwee i man and man and institution and institution,
and which would entitle them to ask for a rearrangement
which would be of benefit both to the shareholders and
the depositors.” All “which” appears to distinctly
imply that the Commercial of Australia doesn’t see its
way to release its deposits before maturity, as so many
other banks have done.
Even despite the acknowledgment made by Mr.
Service that more time might be required, the old man
bluffed as vigorously as ever about the magnificent posi-
tion of the new Commercial, and said that certain recent
rumors concerning it almost amounted to “conspiracy.”
There are certain things about the institution which
ought to make Mr. Service just a shade more humble.
The Commercial stopped the payment of interest on
£2,100,000 of deposits by turning them into preference-
shares which now receive no dividend, and thus the
average interest payable on its fixed deposits is only a
fraction over 3 per cent. But for this saving the new
bank would now show something much more like a loss
than a dividend. Moreover, the increased net profit
for last half-year, which made the institution feel so
wildly self-confident, arose almost wholly out of a vigorous
reduction of expenses, for the earnings of the bank are
painfully small. Tub Bulletin knows of no actual reason
why it Shouldn’t pull through in the end, and sincerely
hopes to see it do so, but as a source of permanent in-
come to the shareholders its present prospects are not re-
assuring.
* * * *
Two years ago James Service lashed himself
into a frenzy of confidence in the Old Bank and
its assets. Two hundred tall, black hats beat the
air for simulated joy when he predicted that prefer-
ence shares in the grand Old Bank reconstructed would
be as negotiable as bank-notes at par. The other day he
was merely on bowing terms with the Old Bank, so to
speak. The ingenious gentleman begged his audience to
remember that he was talking about a New Bank alto-
gether—a beautiful, bright financial infant with liabilities
amounting to only £1,800,000 against assets totting up to
£4,800,000. He asked the meeting if any harm could pos-
sibly come to such a bank, and the meeting smiled super-
ciliously at the bare thought of it. Also James Service
volunteered an assurance (scarcely necessary from a
chairman) that he was speaking the whole truth, and no-
thing but the truth, when he said that the New Board
were not implicated in the Old Bank, which, two
•years before, he had been so proud of. Yet he
■admitted that a golden thread of kinship joined
ithe Old Bank and the New. He was glad, for in-
• stance, to note a great rise (after a greater fall,
• which he did not note) in the price of ‘‘our preference
(Shares, and a considerable rise in the price of our deposit
(receipts.” The humor of it ! Said deposits were in the
•Old Bank, and if the New Bank has only £1,800,000 of
liabilities on its back there was nothing to justify that
(personal pronoun in the possessive case. The joke thick-
ened when James Service said “ there are no liabilities of
sthe Old Bank that mature till a period still to come of
meanly 3J years, so that for 3J years, supposing every-
thing to go wrong at the end of that period, we are abso-
lutely safe.”
• * * •
Ait the Bank of Victoria half-yearly meeting a
(Shareholder “wished to know if a distinction would be
rshown between the affairs of the old bank and the new in
the next balance-sheet.” Edward Miller, in the chair,
ireplied that “ it was all one hank, as the new bank had
taken over the assets and liabilities of the old.” The
■shareholder remarked that the Commercial Bank had
made a very great difference, but the matter dropped
•with a sickening thud. The Bulletin now regards Mr.
lEdward Miller as a lost vessel. He doesn’t understand
ithe ghostly independence of the two commingled Banks
•of Victoria, and he is in danger of having to beg hereafter
■.a. cup of cold water from James Service, who believed and
<was saved.

The B.N.Z. reports a loss of ,£20,416 for the
half-year ended 30th Sept., ’94, and no div. is declared.
The profits, as show n by the balance-sheet issued in March
last, were at the rate of £20,500 for the half-year, so that
there is a change for the had amounting to nearly £41,000.
Part of t his perhaps £lO,OOO or sa—may fairly be set
down to the fact that interest had to be paid on a large
amount of the new State-guaranteed 4 per cent, capital
which could not be safely invested quite as fast as itoame
in, therefore a certain loss was inevitable before the bank
fairly set I led down to its new conditions. The rest of the
lamentable discrepancy seems to be pure disaster. The
note circulation has fallen off by £50,000. Deposits show
a drop of half-a-rnillion. The coin has increased by
£200,000, but the discounts and bills receivable have
dropped by something like £1,000,000, which looks like a
serious decline in business, though it is probably only a
temporary fall caused hv the hank’s s raitened condition
of a few months ago. Also, the “ Investments in British
Consols, &c.,” have increased by £540,000. Making all
possible allowance for the “&c.,” the interest on this
item can hardly exceed 3J per cent, all round, and half-a-
million borrowed at 4 per cent, and invested at 3J is not
calculated to yield an immediate profit. As already fore-
shadowed, £300,000 more is to be written off to cover
losses, and the call of £3 6s. Bd. per share was made on
this account. Having thus got over about ihe most excit-
ing half-year in its history, grave hopes are entertained
that the B.N.Z. will present a b-U'r aspect when
next heard from. Next May, £750,000 worth of the
Estates Co.'s ruinous debentures are to he paid off, and
this alone should cause a perceptible change for the
belter.

Four months ago Madame De Alba got from a
Melbourne jury a verdict.for £BOOO against the Freehold
Investment Co. (in liquidation). The damages were on
account of a lift accident, and Madams fairly won them
after an exhaustive trial. But a busted company with a
B. list of contributories is too good a client for lawyerdom
to abandon to its fate. The other day they had carried
the little matter as far as an appeal to the Pull Court.
The appeal was refused, so the busted company’s legal
advisers may as w’ell draw up a oase to put before the
Fnvy Council, or something of that sort. Also, the un-
happy victims of the Freehold Investment Co., if they
ever assemble together nowadays, may as well ask
for a full statement of legal expenses incurred by not
settling the case out of Court.

The London Bank of Australia, which agreed
to pay its fixed depositors by ten annual instalments, has
now released the second of these four years ahead of
time. One-fifth of the total is thus set free.

The County of Bourke (Melb.) Building Society
has obtained the court’s sanction to an arrangement
whereby the fixed depositors are to receive 2J per cent,
instead of five as heretofore. Judging by the success of
this experiment the return of confidence is eiidently
getting along all right, but the return of cash doesn’t
make much progress.

* * * *
Civil Engineer Frederick B. Gipps is big with a
mighty electrical scheme for illuminating Sydney by
night, and furnishing motive power for anything and
everything by day, with fascinating little accessories of
irrigation by the utilised w-aterfall, and an underground
electric railway along Pitt street from Redfi rn terminus
to Circular Quay. Gipps calculates that it can all he
done, with a £50,000 electric plant and 24 miles of cable
thrown in, for £140,000, and it would be interesting to
have the individual checking of his figures by a round
dozen or so of experts. The Sydney Corporation is now
endeavoring to get an electric-lighting Bill through Par-
liament, so that Gipps is determined to be well in time
with his proposals. His scheme includes the construction
of a storage reservoir of 600,000,000 gallons at Kenny Hill;
the tunnelling of the dividing-range by a 4-raile tunnel
carrying 30in. pipes, and giving a fall of 256 ft. in the
distance, the overflow of the water on turbine-w heels, etc.,
the waste to he nsed afterwards in irrigating the valley.

It is said that a beautiful Vic. estate, the home
of a most celebrated racehorse, for which £75,000 was
supposed to have been paid in boom-time, has, with all
its splendid improvements, come back to its original
owner. At least £20,000 was paid off the purchase-money
by the now-insolvent purchaser.
* ♦ * »
The new Hampton Plains Estate C©., which
has just acquired the concession originally granted to the
H.P. syndicate by the liberal Forrest crowd, announces
that it is ready to receive offers for the exploitation of its
property. It doesn’t propose to do anything itself; but
it cheerfully consents to sit at ’Ome and fob the earnings
of its sub-contractors, w T ho will sweat the prospectors,
who will sweat in search of gold. This is a very gratifying
state of things, and shows how emphatically right the
Yilgarn miners were in returning a Parliamentary repre-
sentative to support the gang in office.

Baker's Creek Co., the Eleanora’s elder sister,
still prospers. It is about the only steady-dividend-paying
N.S.W. gold-mine. During the past half 5677 tons ore
gave 88220zj. gold, and 21 tons of concentrates 96ozs. gold
and 1350z5. silver. The percentage of gold shows a slight
decrease. Management and wages come to £12,471155. 9d.;
£15,000 has been paid in divs, ; present year begins
£7974 16s. Bd. to credit. Since (in ’B7) the mine started,
45,500 tons of ore,have given over 3J tons of gold, and
£307,500 has been paid to the shareholders, mostly Vic-
torian and S.A. speculators.
After all the praise for years lavished on the
square-set timbering in Broken Hill, it is now admitted
by the directors themselves that had it never been
used and the mine always worked, as now, on the
open-out system, another million, at least, would have
been distributed in divs. A million of money to-day
represents more than one-half the value of the mine,
whieh has a reserve of o\ er half-a-million. Tub Bulletin
said, years ago, that a big lode that is not filled in would
in time fill itself. Had the first American manager come
from a hig silver-lead mine at Leadyille, instead of from
Comstock, where the veins are small, there would have
been no square-sets, and the shareholders would have had
their million of monev.

12-heod battery, at Bulgandra, 40 miles from Alhury, “Is
expected to average over an oz.” The writer, who
recently sampled it, would like to wager it won’t average
9dwts.
• * » *
The 50-head at Homeward Bound, Yalwal,
(N.S.W.), is idle, pending the settlement of a jumping
ease. The management omitted the payment of £5 and
the mine was jumped. After two decisions in favor of
the jumpers the case is now going to Equity.
* * » »
After six months’ resultleas work prospecting in
Kerr’s Freehold, trying to pick up a continuation of the
Lucknow gold, shareholders get from Harry Newman the
great consolation that they are only in their infancy.
They have any quantity of area, and there is no “abso-
lute reason ” why feeders and results similar to Lucknow
should not occur.
* * • *
_ Two prospectors report finding native quick-
silver near Lismore, N.S.W., but cannot find the lode.
Very likely. It may be miles away. No metal can travel
like quicksilver.

ing, 188 oz. from 150 tons. What about the 3000-ton heap
of two to three oz. stone now? In a few short months
Bayley’s will have joined the “pass-outs.”

The New Bonang now seems to bo on its last
legs. The main shaft has been deepened 100 ft. since the
mine made its last start two years ago, hut has revealed
nothing. Over £lOO,OOO of gold has been taken out dur-
ing the last 28 years, and several companies have spent
their capital in getting it.

In point of gold-yield the Towers is the fore-
most city in Australia. Ballarat comes second with
193,OOOoz.—employing 7000 miners. The Towers, how-
ever, proudly boasts that it employs only half the number
of men, and pays only one-fourth as much in calls.

Crude kerosene is alleged to be flowing from a
bore at East Moree (N.S.W.). If anyone could discover a
hig oil district—what a boom there would be !
* * • •
The idea of pumping water from Esperance Bay
to Coolgardie is revived. It is, anyhow, more sensible
than Mercer’s idea of erecting a 1000-head mill on the
Swan, and running railways to the different fields. It is
cheaper to pump water than carry ore and build railways.
Proposed to lay a 220-mile line of 14-inch pipes with
pumping stations every 20 miles.
• * ♦ •
Warden Finnerty having ruled in favor of the
dry blowers of Hannan’s and White Feather, the next
step of the lessees will probably be to claim compensation
from Govt, for granting leases and allowing the diggers to
remain in possession.

is described as “ not suitable for a battery "—a novel way
of admitting that it is barren.

with it.

Mulligan’s Reward, the P.O. on Mount Carey,
The Bulletin hears, “is a large body of ore containing a
lot of copper and iron and apparently a fair quantity of
gold. Wilson’s shows, found by Heflernan and Hall about
two miles from there, are not very rich, but they are
working away at them. There is a big reef at the Hawk’s
Nest carrying fair gold, hut wanting development. There
are many other shows about Mount Margaret and Lake
Carey, but none of them very wonderful.”
