Dear Bulletin, —Re that Ivanhoe crushing.
Manager Hooker was not in charge at the time it was
made. He came up later, and the figures he gives are
second-hand from an old report. The Otis mill referred
to is on Boulder East and was working night and day
almost continuously for the two months, and all who saw
the report made calculations as to bow only 80 tong could
have been treated i,|i,that time. I remember asking the
boy who fed the battery, and it wasjaeyond hie arithmetic,
too. —Yours, &c., Yo-Ho.
• * * *
The “Mystery ” mine, near One, is well-named.
Two years ago it was pegged by a man who left it to go
prospecting. Then a man named Moss dollied a lot of
gold, and left for other fields. Then it successively had
four owners, all of whom deserted it. Finally, the original
prospector regained possession, and worked the minei
until one morning he was found at the bottom of the
shaft with his throat ctit, his neck broken and a gash
across one wrist. Whether it was murder or suicide is a
mystery, but the question is—Will the mine ever bring
anyone any luck? It is now under offer in London,
♦ * * *
The cement-rush at White Feather, from which
leaseholders have been trying to drive tire men, is theonly
genuine alluvial on the field. The gold is water-worn and
distinct from the usual igneous “ slugs ” and reef-gold
which pass for alluvial. The sinking is from 15 to 20ft.—
blasting all the way; and as there is one hole to every
fifty square feet there is quite a fusillade of “shots” at
times. The wash, which consists of rounded quartz pebbles,
is dollied on flat stones and then dry-blown. But it is not
all gold-bearing, only in streaks. One man got gold ten
fact down. He refused £2OO down for his claim because
he expected to have the wash from that depth down to
the general 20ft. But next day came a bar thinning it
down to a couple of feet, and he only got an ounce or
two.

Things looking sick at Ninety Mile, although it

♦ * * •
There is in the Bonang district a mine run on
the coolie-labor principle. Miners are working for 30s. a
week, and surface-hands for 15s. to £l, and “tucker”
themselves. Yet they have fair gold and a decent reef.
* * * •
While mining leases limited to small areas dis-
courage the expenditure of capital necessary for the
erection of machinery, leases of large areas often shut out
bona-fide miners. The remedy seems to be indicated pro-
perly in a resolution passed at the last Miners’ Confer-
ence : “That no leases should be issued on any new gold-
field for three years after discovery’’; and then, after
three years, let the areas of leases be more commensurate
with the needs of the capital required to develop pro-
blematical properties.
• • * *
Albert Calvert has just published a “West
Australian Mining Manual and Guide to Investors.” After
having got rid of the properties he was interested in, more
or less unsuccessfully, he has now elected to become the
guide, philosopher and friend of the British public, and
to warn them against bogus companies.

out her paddock, wheels the dirt, washes it, and makes
her ten ’weights a week as well as any man around her.

The success of the John Woods Co., Stawell,
has cheated a local boom. In the Keats Block claim
heavy gold has been strudk. Promoters’ shares were at
once issued at £1 a share, and sold for £4 same evening.
A co. has since been formed, represented by 10,200 shares.

The Union Co. still holds the big end of the
M.L. trade, principally because it runs such large ships as
the Monowai. During its last 17 days’ round-trip from
Sydney to Melbourne, over 900 saloon passengers ate on
the one-time American liner.
» * • *
The New Costerseld Metal Extraction Co.,
Vic., run by the plucky Bob Beeston, has not turned up
trumps after ail. It was all in the tailings at the start
and, as .usual, remains there at the finish. The treatment
was right enough if there had been only a little more
gold.
♦ ♦ • *
Melb. streets look very peculiar with the-engines,
poppet-heads and winding-gear of the sewage shafts.
The other day the man on the windlass at Piinders-st.
was approached by a beery individual who said : “ Where
are yer pegs, mate ?” The sewage braceman didn’t under-
stand and told him to clear out. He did, but growled
“ You must he a new-chum, anyhow, if you think there’B
gold in that stuff.”
* * • *
According to P.O. Fysh, of Tasmania, Aus-
tralia pays in all £168,000 per annum to London bankers
for “managing” the public debt, said management con-
sisting mostly of paying the half-yearly divs. to bond-
holders. Wanted badly, an amalgamated Agent-General
for all Australia, with a staff of clerks who will keep the
stock-registers for the various provinces, and write out
cheques on the Government account for the divs. Even
if the amalgamated A.-G. got £5OOO a year, and the staff
was constructed on a lavish system, there should he room
to save £150,000 annually.
♦ ♦ * *
Sundry churches and pious institutions in
Maoriland are holders of Bank of N.Z. shares, mostly
donated to them in the good old days when the B.N.Z.
was founded on a rock, and .the glory of Vogelism hadn’t
yet departed. The call of £3 6s. Bd. per share has been a
fearful knock to the tabernacle.

In excusing the small div. paid by Balmain Co-
operative Ferry Co. the chairman said shareholders should
recollect “it was not a gold-mine.” Times have changed,
for such an illustration was never made in that sense in
Sydney a few years ago.
A plant for treating blood, offal and waste
products generally is being erected at the Riverina
Frozen Meat Works, at Deniliquin (N.S.W.). This
machinery, which is from the Austral Otis Engineering
Works, Melbourne, will convert all the elements, which
were previously a very aggressive nuisance, into a com-
mercial dry manure—a product for which there is ample
steady demand throughout Australasia. This is the
climax of scientific economy—the utilisation of all
natural material without waste. The plant, which is very
complete, is automatic in all its operations.*

affair) was being capitally tried, he suddenly
accused Mr. Rogers of smiling at his misery, and
the accusation, which was quite untrue, cut
Rogers to the quick.

When a certain gritty
and pugnacious Queensland
politician was P.M. on a Far
N.Q. field, lie and Simpson,
the local knocker-out, were
having a good go-in just out-
side tiie chief pub, when a
policeman made his appear-
ance, collared his superior’s
adversary, and marched him
off to “chokee.” The case
came on in due time, the
P.M., of course, on the
bench. Prisoner pleaded
guilty ; Robert, grinning,
gave his evidence. The P.M.
put his hand into his trousers
pocket, drew out two half-
crowns, and planked them
down on the desk in front of
him. Then he turned to the
prisoner. “ The decision of
this bench, sir,” he said,
sternly, “is that you be
fined five shillings. And
now, Simpson,” continued
the magnate, stepping cheer-
fully down from his seat,
“ come into the back yard
and we’ll finish it.”

One of the oldest N.S. Wales lI.C. pen-
sioners, Dean Hanley, died on Sunday at Five
Dock, aged 80 years. Eleven R.C. clergymen
sl.ill draw salaries from the State, amounts ranging
for 41200 to £lOO per annum. The oldest N.S. W.
clerical pensioner is Rev. F. W. Addams, who
has drawn 41200 a year since 1846.
Huxley, one of the lucky Londonderry
men, was found to be suffering from typhoid on
the arrival of the Arcadia at Port Adelaide, and
is now very ill at the residence of “Jim” Shaw
(Mayor of Coolgardie) at Kent Town (S.A.).
Huxley was ailing all the voyage, and the fever
definitely developed a few hours after he arrived.

Sladen’s “The Japs at Horne,” now in a fifth
edition, is “tiie very worst book ever written on
Japan,” and that tiie success of it and of the
books and articles on Japan, written by A. H.
Savage Lander (tie was in Australia lately),
tends to show that only lying pays literary
dividends.
A pastoralist, who lately got a nullity-of-
marriage decree against tiie woman who had
(thinking her marriage perfectly legal) lived
with him for many years as his wife, and was
very properly bustled by the Court into making
the discarded lady a substantial monetary allow-
ance, has now, it is understood, married another
governess.
The Rev. Turner, Particular Baptist minis-
ter, who died at Melbourne the other day, after 45
years’ work in the little conventicle at the corner
of Stephen and Lonsdale streets, years before his
death let the churchyard to a tombstone-maker.
So that the church is to this day surrounded by
noble monuments, with never a corpse beneath
one of them.
Gladstone’s postcard to Parkes wishing
him a happy new year, was, considering Parkes’
outlook, like the joke of an enemy. But again,
Parkes handed over the Gladstone communication
to the daily press, whereas there is no mention of
Gladstone’s having similarly handed over Parkes’
carefully-worded and flattering letter on beauti-
fully-embossed vellum. .
The time has arrived for certain land-
boomers, of the twopence ha’penny in the £ class,
to get their “broken fortunes” mended by mys-
terious legacies from unheard-of relatives. Opulent
great-aunts, and wealthy cousins many times re-
moved. are expected to expire shortly, leaving
large sums of money to sundry unfortunate specu-
lators who have been lying low.
Dwyer Gray, who has come to Australia
“to stay,’’had two close relatives here before him.
Wilson Gray, the father of Victorian Democracy,
who came to Australia in the same ship with
Charles Gavan Duffy, in ’56, was an uncle; and
another uncle was a medico who travelled at one
time for the Mutual Life office, with Tennant
Donaldson as canvasser. Another uncle is Col.
Chisholm, P.M. at Yass ; Dvyer Gray’s mother
being a daughter of Mrs. Chisholm, the “Immi-
grants’ Friend.”

Miss MURIEL GARR, the lay nine of the Roselle-Darre Co.
Mr. Justice Stephen (N.S.W.) did not
start on his six months’ leave until after his two
months’ vacation—which means that the leave
dates from the last day of vacation, and shows
that His Honor glues a cunning blue-eye on the
patient taxpayer.
The Sydney banquet to politician Slattery
last week was attended by Cardinal Moran and
his numerous inevitable entourage. So many of
them were there, in fact, that a wag thought of
addressing the audience as “Friends, Romans,
countrymen ! ”
Capt. Gascoigne was a mystery in Sydney
and Melb. naval and commercial circles, a quarter
of a century ago. He had a clever son, and a
schooner yacht. They went on voyages to the
South Seas, presumably, and came back flush of
money, so that some fancied it was gold, and
others a touch of Bully Hayes. At last they dis-
appeared for ever, schooner and all.
Cowra (N.S.W.) boasts the possession of a
gradually - dissolving patriarch named James
Sharpe, 106 years old, twice widowered, and the
father of 15 children. Sharpe’s father lived to be
129, and his mother 124—50 he says, the gay
romancer. However, James is very, very old, and
attributes it largely to the fact that he was alway'
a “fair ” drinker and a heavy smoker.
Premier Forrest (W.A.), the autocratic
boss and personal validator of mining-jumps,
declares that, in the matter of the mail-steamers
calling at Fremantle or Albany, he “ wishes to
avoid Sunday labor at the West Australian port
of call.” ’Twas ever thus. The Tory, owning
the earth and the slave that tills the earth,
was always the prize Pharisee, the rigid Sabba-
tarian of his day and generation. Good old
piety !
Parkes has prepared a yarn for his tomb-
stone, they say. He will be described as a direct
descendant of Faulconbridge, the left-handed son
of Richard Coeur de Lion, and the epitaph will
conclude with a verse from an olde Englyshe
ballade :
Ye King upon ye babe didde gaze
And passe these fevve remarks
“Thy royale blood in distante days
S lall fille ye veins of Parkes.”
Lloyd Osbourne, in his memorial volume
recounting the death of Robert Louis Stevenson; —
He lay in the ball which was ever his pride, where he
had passed the gayest and most delightful hours of his
life, a noble room with open stairway and mullioned
windows.
People generally understand that the novelist
lived and died in a free-and-easy sort of nigger-
built bamboo house. “Mullioned windows!”
Why weren’t they “oriel ” windows? Mr. Lloyd
Osbourne is not so much a novelist as he is the
stepson of a novelist.

ney, Mr. James Martin”—afterwards Chief
Justice—“may attain a respectable position (he
has on one or two occasions made a lucky hit in
tiie Police Court) but his position has not been
tli it of a public man, neither is he trained for a
senator. A man who may cut a tolerable figure
iu argument before a police-magistrate may make
a sorry exhibition in debate before the Speaker

The Cootamundra (N.S.W.) “mystery”
is apparently founded on one of the most hideous
of murders. The victim’s body was actually cut
open and stuffed with stone to make it sink.
The supply of beef from British cattle last
year was 721,900 tons, against 273,700 tons im-
ported, and the mutton from British flocks was
322,000 tons, against 127,917 tons imported.
THOMAS CARLYLE ON SMOKING.
The eminent Scotch philosopher, who was as good!
a judge of a cigar as of a book, used to say that
amongst habits which grow on men, none imposes
itself more than smoking.” But, strange to say,
in order to derive the maximum enjoyment out of
the weed an occasional change in the style of your •
smoke is indispensable. To continue for any
length of time the same brand of cigar or tobacco >
causes the smoker to grow so accustomed to it,,
that his palate gets indifferent to its special blend;.
With a view to meet the, general desire for ai
mild and aromatic smoke, Messrs. J. Frossard andl
Co. have lately brought out their Cavour Cigars ;
they are beautifully packed in neat cases of eight
to be retailed at one shilling. This article fills a
long-felt want for a light smoke, possessing flavor
and delicate aroma, and is certainly the best
value cigar in the market. Once tried, always
liked.

bluntly prophesied. It would be a stroke of rare
good luck for Eyeglass’s future if an immediate
adverse vote robbed- him of this chance of pleasing
his wife.
Strange contradiction ! Queensland won’t
let her Northern Territory separate, while South
Australia is anxious to get rid of hers, and has
appointed seven M’s.L.A. to consult with the
Governor thereupon.

“That point was decided in 1873 by the Speaker.”
A member: “What Speaker?” Mason: “Sir
Charles M'Mahon.” Another member : “What
time of the night was it?”
Significant that, almost without exception,
the South Australian-born sons and daughters of
Germans resent the name of Germans and demand
to be called Australians. The Imperial festivals
ind fete-days are all abandoned to the elders save
when an attractive dance or concert follows.

The N.S.W. Protectionists who rejoice
because the Reid Government retains their tariff,
are very short-sighted. Should the duties be
repealed as prosperity returns, the cause of Pro-
teotion would be damned for years. r i he average
elector, instead of being able to detect an undis-
tributed middle term, is apt to be entrapped by
tlie fallacy post hoc propter hoc. At the general
election the Dibbs Ministry was condemned for
a state of affairs resulting from the bank smashes,
the stoppage of borrowed money, the labour-
troubles, and other things, for which it was
not responsible. There was method in Parkes’
apparent madness when he flung up_ office to let
his successors pay the penalty of his own folly
and incompetency.
A noticeable feature of the N.S.W. vine-
growers’ conference was the body’s thorough
accord re the fiscal character of the ameliorations
sought. Differential wine and brandy duties as
against outsiders were taken as a prime lever.
The beginning of interprovincial Freetrade
seems to exist in the agreement (made subject to
Parliamentary approval) by which Maoriland is to
admit South Australian fruit, wine, olive oil, and
salt duty-free in return for a similar concession to
Maoriland oats, barley, horses, and hops.
M‘Millau’s contemplated trip to England
suggests the possibility of his temporarily taking
charge of N.S. W. Agent-Generalship if Reid can
perfect his notions on that matter. Saul Samuel
will, in the latter event, retire on leave, and the
interval till the arrival of the new man will, it is
mooted, be at M'Millan’s dispjsal.

ings of the Democratic Club on Sunday nights.

Heligoland ! The fires of Australian Democracy
will shortly consume to ashes the Forrests, the
Jlacketts, the Thynnes, the Archers, and the
Byrneses. •

A newspaper linotype office intimated
lately thai in the near future most of the hands
employed would be women and girls. This is
Nemesis once more. Not long ago several of the
Typographical Associations declined to allow
women or girls to set type in unionist workshops
as fellow-employes with them. Now, out go the
masterful wearers of trousers and in go petticoats
as bosses of the field. But female cheapness
would have prevailed anyhow.

ing to ex-Pretnier Parkes’ nasty remark that he
believed that he (Reid) had never read a law-
book, pertinently enquires how it was then that
Parkes, when Premier, offered him (Reid) the
Attorney-Generalship. Reid remarks that Parkes
onoe also offered him the Speakership, and he is
proud to be able to say that he always refused to
accept office under Parkes aforesaid. Reid
further points to a three-year-old Parkesian speech

Adelaide Corporation recently decided, by
a narrow majority, to abolish day-lator in all
municipal work and to call for tenders. This
means a saving of about .£5OO a year direct from
the pockets of the workers. The new idea is a
triumph lor the National Ass party, who, when
defeated at the last S. A. elections, ran candidates
for the municipal bodies, and Fuller, M.L.C., and
Tomkinson, ex-M.L.C., were elected aldermen.
These two high Tories are at the bottom of the
cheap-labor reform now to be tried.
From Sydney D.T.’s report of N.S.W.
fruit conference :
Mr. J. Sutton, . . . although a Freetrader, warned
the Government against the danger of removing the duties
from dried fruits until the people had had an opportunity
of cultivating a taste for the local article.
“Although a Freetrader” is most excellent in
this conjunction. It illustrates the fact that the
average Freetrader is prepared to concede the
whole Protectionist ticket, provided that it suits
his own business. But he still calls himself a Free-
trader.
Almost the last official act of the historic
Fitz Gibbon, of the Melb. Board of Works, before
the recent lamentable attempted reduction of his
salary, was to vote against tiie establishment of a
6s. per day minimum wage for the employes of
contractors under the Board, because it would
increase the cost of a certain piece of work from
.£1940 to £2OOO. When the Assembly was dis-
cussing the proposed reduction of Fitz Gibbon’s
own minimum wage, and he came round with a
forbidding expression of countenance to see how
the debate was getting on, people remembered his
own enthusiastic advocacy of cheapness at any
price, and smiled. Why shouldn’t the Board get
its Fitz Gibbon at the lowest possible wage as well
as its pick-and-shovel gentlemen?
Archibald Archer, M.P., the Federal
Councillor who squeaked out —“ Queensland will
not submit to the abolition of Kanaka labor,” is
the senile politician who recently received from
his Rockhampton constituents the most unanimous
request to resign on record. He was M‘ll wraith’s
first Treasurer, and showed such Conspicuous in-
competence, that his first term of office was his last,
and people thanked God for it. He persuaded
himself that he was a C.Q. Separatiomst, and got a
trip to England on the strength of his persuasion;
but he has since changed his mind at the instiga-
tion of the gang in office, and now acts as a wet
blanket to his colleague Curtis’s enthusiasm.
Archer, like Parkes, trades on his white hair ; and
will assuredly be thrown out upon it by the
enraged electors very first chance that offers.
Perhaps the finest specimen of the N.S.W.
special breed of State White Elephant is the just-
opened Marrickville-to-Burwood-Road railway.
It is only five miles long and traverses a small
strip of country—sparsely populated and alto-
gether desolate—between the Suburban and Illa-
warra lines, and close to the classic regions around
Cook’s River. The construction and land-re-
sumption charges reach the respectable total of
<£162,000, or over =£32,000 a mile, and never, by
the wildest stretch of imagination, can the line be
made to pay interest on capital. The only pos-
sible occasion when the route is likely to be
decently patronised will be on race-days at Can-
terbury Park; otherwise, the locality could
hardly support a line of coaches, let alone
a railway. Last week, when a train of six
carriages reached Redfern, there were only
three passengers, including two dead - heads,
and, on the same day, an outward train
contained one solitary second-class passenger.
And yet the Department—the politicians, not the
Department, are responsible for the line’s con-
struction—runs 24 trains a day up and down. One
railway man calculates that the revenue received
should just about pay the cost of grease on the
rolling-stock. In the end, it is asserted, the line
will be closed altogether, or a single horse and car
employed only. Had this enormous amount of
money been spent in laying light lines in good
agricultural districts, it would have been of im-
mense benefit, and returns would have been
assured. At present those benefited are only
they whose land has been resumed.
Queensland Government, at the Boil-
citation of certain pastoralista, sank a bore
in the town of Barcaldine. Result: a mag-
nificent supply of water, which the Govern-
raent proceeded to utilise by clapping thereon
a prohibitive tariff. Protests from the towns-
people were all in vain. Whereupon the
citizens put down a bore of their own, got a splen-
did water-supply, and the Government bore
became the Fed white elephant.
N.S. W. political leaders are
now almost unanimous in prophesying
an appeal to the electors in or before
July next.
Under Freetrade, Great Britain
is said to accumulate annually 65 mil-
lions sterling. Many experts say that
since 1876 she has been 1 sing instead
of gaining. Under Protection America
accumulates annually ICS millions,
and France 75 millions. Under Free-
trade England imports 130 millions
"sterling more than she exports;
Protective America exports more than
she imports. Under Freetrade the
commerce of England has increased
21 per cent, in' 10 years. Under Pro-
tection the commerce of the United
States has increased 68 per cent, in
the same period, Holland 57 percent.,
Belgium 51 per cent., France 39 per
cent.
A Southern M.P. was lately
addressing his constituents _on the
work of the session. An elector asked
him how it was he was away from
Parliament on one occasion for over
a wee£ “ Well,” said the M.P., “I
was detained by urgent business, and
I—er ” The elector rose. “What
kind of business?” he asked. “ Ob-
it—was—of a political nature!” stam-
mered the M.P. “Now look here,
old boy,” said the questioner, “can
you tell me why you have so much
political business to transact at Mrs.
’s in street?” The member
subsided. Subsequently the elector
was asked how he came to know the
facts. “ It’s like this,” he said; “I
was with him all that week, and, by
, we had a real good time 1”

and Bowden, publishers) is a new sixpenn’orth of
weak art and second-rate literature after the
model of the Strand, and the first number appears

middle-class taste and capacity. The preface
grovels in devotion to the respectable German
middle-class lady who squats on the English
1 ll ™ne, and pictures of royalty and alleged ansto-
craey are scattered with a liberal hand, ihe

of the leisurely trail of the tract (neat phrase);

tie sycophant, ihe quantity of theWiNPSOR is
(like that of Queen Victoria) admirable.

A large Melb. drapery firm has announced
its intention of refusing employment to members
of women’s franchise leagues, and the various Yic.
leagues are very mad about it. It now remains
for a smart rival firm to declare in favor of the
leagues and scoop the knickerbocker trade.
# * •
There was a little mild excitement at
a Sydney tailor’s last week, when two “new
women” arrived to order a suit. The material
having been chosen, a cutter blandly came forward
with his tape-measure. They submitted to the
waist-measuring, but when it came to getting the
figures for the knickers, they asked, somewhat
confusedly, if there was not some arrangement—
er— by which the length of the arm would deter-
mine the size of the leg. On being assured that
there wasn’t, matters began to look serious, when
the proprietor suggested that if they stepped up to
the workroom a female employe would perform
the necessary tape-work. They seized the idea,
and left quite satisfied.
# # *
“It is the cheese” in Melbourne among
the gilded knickerbocker women to cycle forth
followed, at a distance of six paces, by a gorgeous
groom on a well-oiled bicycle. The New Woman,
when she is really “ New,” does not approve of a
bicycle built for two. For one thing, somebody
would have to be in front, and the New Woman
would, of course, allow no man precedence. And
if, on the contrary, the man were behind, he
would be- bound to notice that the New Woman
was usually not quite so new as she ought to be.
For it is into a woman’s throat and the back of
her neck that Time, the destroyer, first fastens his
remorseless t ;eth. A worn in’s face always tells
tales, but her neck !
* * *
Cycling is to be the medium for the exten-
sion of the breeches-idea among Melb. women,
who find it the most convenient method of justify-
ing their shedding of skirts.
# * #
It is expected that the great “M‘Meckan
will-case” will shortly be ended. The solicitor,
who has been residing in England for the past two
years on behalf of plaintiff (appeal having been
made to the Privy Council), has just returned to
Melbourne. The property, which was valued at
between <£150,000 and .£160,000 when Capt.
M'Meckan died, has much increased in value, not
a penny having been invested in bung banks or
i Mated property-values. The plaintiff, “Jack”
M‘Meckan, chief officer of the Monowai, is
engaged to a very charming South Yarra young
lady, the daughter of a well-known Victorian
engineer.
Very early the other morn-
ing, at Spencer-street station, a
suburban doctor’s wife tried her
hand at “New-Woman” tactics.
Half-an-hour earlier she had seen
her luggage aboard, and gone up
town for breakfast, returning (as
she thought, in time) for her up-
country train. But it was just
starting, and the porters shut the
gate, refusing to letler through
without a ticket. Nothing
daunted, hhe vaulted sheer over
the barrier, and took a bee-:'iine
for the guard’s-van, while hubby
gaped in astonishment outside in
the cold, and thanked the gods for
his noble wife. Unfortunately,
she was intercepted short of the
goal, and had to forego her trip,
for that day at all events.. The
fat fell in the fire when a red-tape
tyrant threatened to make her
leave the enclosure in the same
way as she had come in.
Which recalls the former prin-
cipal of a high-toned Melb. sec-
tarian college. Strolling in the
back garden, he occasionally
detected one or other of his young
lady pupils getting out of the
window of the music-room in
which she was supposed to be
practising. Upon these occasions
it was his custom to hasten to
the scene and request the culprit
to climb in again, which she had
to do while he stood coldly and
critically by.
• . * *
The haunts of Justice have
much attraction for criminals.
Some men frequent the police-
court galleries daily, year in and
year out. It is the very circum-
stance of their constant attend-
ance which often gets them into
trouble. Case in pointCourt-
sergeant (loq.) : “That fellow’s
been here every Id ay for the last
six months, and he always annoys
me with his horrible grin. Must
bring him up under the vag.”
Next day the man figures in court
again—this time in the dock. Fact.
The Victorian Govt., which won’t take
money for the registration of the temples of Venus
(on the ground that —though that might lessen
disease —it would debase the State), hesitates not
to pocket the fines exacted with increasing regu-
larity from the wretched women of the town.
Every morning, at Melb. City Court, the number
of women charged as “ solicitors ” —for prostitutes
are of two kinds, legal and illegal—is equal to
that of the drunks, and in nearly every case a
fine of five shillings or more is exacted. Since the
beginning of the year one woman has been at the
bar no less than eight times, and the State has
received from her the average sum of six shillings
a week. Thus the State occupies the delectable
position of a pensioner upon the gains of prostitu-
tion.
# # *
Chief Secretary Bruuker’s readjustment of
N.S.W. police travelling expenses saves, taking a
batch at a time, 2s. in the <£, and, besides, pro-
portions more reasonably than heretofore the
relations of man toman. Under the old scheme,
a travelling superintendent was supposed to spend
£1 per day, whereas a travelling constable was cut
down to 4s. There is no part of the Australian
bush where diversities of table and lodging are
such as to take in two men together at these
figures, and as superintendents generally travel
with a couple of men, either the superintendent
pocketed half his allowance or the constables had
to sleep behind a log. Fifteen shillings for a
super, and ss. for a constable (Brunker’s scheme)
will probably work out thus : —Superintendent:
3 meals and bed, 10s., wine, 55.; Bobby ; 3 meals
and bed, 45., beer, Is.
# # *
The fair in connection with St. Pat.’s
(Melb.) Cathedral Fund will re-open at the
Exhibition next month, ostensibly to draw the
art-union. As the lottery has been open for some
months, and the supply of tickets is unlimited,
the chances are something like 3,000,000 to one
against the winner, who will probably turn out to
be a Chinaman or, worse still, an Orangeman.

Of late The Bulletin has taken to think-
ing again of Matthew D.ivies and his insolvency
release which was granted by Chief Justice Mad-
den, and his utter impecuniosity when he had
to defend himself in the Police-court because he
couldn’t afford to pay barristers, and his little
Masonic dinner whereat he spoke of re-attaining
“the top of the tree,” and of the people who put
money into his banks ; also, of many other things
relating to the Davies scandal. The subject has
acquired new social interest.
» # *
Nurseryman Gelding (N.S.W.) explains
the permanence of the cabbagee Chinaman thus :
“ We have been unable to get rid of the Chinese
market-gardener, simply because women prefer a
Chinaman’s ‘twiddle-twaddle’ to making their
purchases in a business-like way with European
vendors.” There is a hatful of truth in this.
The average woman likes the Chow’s eternal,
meaningless, “childlike and bland ” smile ; likes
the patience and “no-savee” with which he pos-
sesses his Oriental soul; likes the chaffing he
stands imperturbably; likes to call him “John,’’
and even to refer to him among her friends as
“My John;” likes to haggle over a peck of
potatoes and to fancy that John gives in to
her superior huckstering ability; and likes the
“Chinese nuts,” and the stray banana that he
gives to the children ; and regularly accepts the
preserved ginger that he leaves as a Christmas-
box. Ugh ! Woman is at the bottom of the
Chinese agony in Australia. Will the “New
Woman ” mend this evil ?

* * *
The Australienne, having got her medical
diploma, thought she would like to settle into a
practice at Darmstadt. The society was delicious,
but her resources ran low, and she felt that some
bold stroke was needed for an advt. Luckily
Prince D., the Russian, came there, and Cast eyes
upon her. He was a w’retched little weed, in the
lowest of health, troubled by insomnia, when she
met him. By professional hypnotism, learnt of Dr.
Charcot, she gave him sleep, and he thus picked
up health amazingly. Then he proposed marriage
to her, but got a nasty knock. She sent him a
receipt for all her fees, and then there appeared
a minute account of the case in the newspaper,
redounding to her credit, but making him look
rather small.
* * *
In the Melb. Photographic Exhibition is a
photogravure of a French painting, representing
a roguish-looking and very pretty girl reclining on
a couch, her cheek resting on her hand, and her
eyes twinkling with an unmistakeable fire. The
artist, in accordance with the passion expressed in
his work, fitted it like a glove with the title,
“Temptation,” but the pious Melb. firm which
exhibits the picture has taken upon itself to
ilowdlerise this into “Reverie,” which it certainly
is not. The Yankee Sehoolmarm who put pants
on the piano isn’t in it with some of the Southern
saints.
But the amateur photo, fiends, on the other
hand, are mostly' godless youths, and among the
exhibits of a certain newspaper of photographs
sent in for its competitions is the picture of a
young lady, “mid nodings on,” reclining on the
mossy turf at the foot of a cluster of tree-ferns.
The girl is pretty and shapely, but her pose is so
poorly conceived and ill-arranged that she only
remains as a subject for Social Gimletists to
indicate for the operation of the flogging clauses
of the Crimes Act.
Regarding the same photo., or one of similar
character, a yarn is told. The artist chose the
model from a number of professional applicants,
and accompanied by her and his faithful camera
made for a picturesqueand unfrequented dell out by
Fern Tree Gully, which he had had in his eye for
some time. On arriving there the lady was placed
in position, and was just being focussed properly,
when a crackling of twigs caused the photographer
to remove his head from the velvet cloth. An
aged man in a belltopper and shirt-sleeves was
approaching with a billy in his hand, and, as there
seemed no way of turning the intruder back, the
artist approached him and made a clean breast.
“ Look here, mister, I’m taking a photo, of a girl
behind those ferns, but I don’t want to be dis-
turbed, so I’ll be obliged if you say nothing.”
“Me !” replied the venerable one; “not me say
anything. But I’ll stop ’n have a look.” And he
followed the man of collodion into the bower, of
Venus. Regarding the divinity for a space of
five seconds, he seated himself on his upturned
billy and remarked, “Well, I’m an ole man and
I’ve lived some years, but bless me if this ain’t
the best picter I ever see in my life.”
* * #
Hobart Exhibition is just now an excellent
place to sleep in, the visitors being exceedingly
few. Day after day the only sounds are the band
playing to the audience of itself, and the fat voice
of the peddler who trades in grease-extractor and
indelible pencils. The Victorian Court and British
Art Gallery are the show, and as most visitors
have no lasting admiration for English art or
Yarraville-rnade boilers, the result is not so
colossal as prophet Joubert said it would be.
Also, the visitor is unable to see the alleged views
from the dome, by reason of the cafe-bar towels
drying thereon. Altogether ’tis a sweet pro-
menade of deadbeats, cheap barmaids, and year-
old whisky.
The sensational Westralian unnatural-
offence case, in which the accused is a newly-
arrived scion of a well-known English family, and
closely related to a somewhat prominent poli-
tician, concluded most abruptly in the discharge
of the prisoner owing to the Bench refusing the
application for a third remand. The popular
impression is that had the defendant been a
laborer the police could have had 40 remands to
round-up their witnesses.
At a Sydney China-
man’s funeral, the other day,
deceased’s friends, wishing
to conduct the obsequies with
all due pomp and ceremony,
engaged a fife - and - drum
band. The latter, unac-
quainted with any dead-
march, substituted “ Home,
Sweet Home ’’ as the dole-
fidlest ditty in their reper-
toire. The sorrowing caval-
cade kept solemn step and
noticed nothing wrong. Then
the instrumentalists got tired
of their theme and struck
boldly into “Two Little
Girls in Blue,” and de-
ceased’s friends quickened
their steps a little and shook
hands with themselves over
the versatility of their band.

If women are going to
take to wearing knicker-
bockers as a general rule,
much leg-padding will have
to be used. Thus Lady Cohn
Campbell, prompted by see-
ing a ladies’ gymnastic dis-
play in London:—“lt is
rather sad to have to record
that in all the band of girls
there is not one good pair of
legs. There are long girls
with legs like cedar pencils ;
there are short ones with
legs like those of a dachs-
hund ; there are legs with
the calves slipped down to
the ankles; and there are
legs without any calves at
all. But among those nine-
teen or twenty girls, fpicked
at random, there is not one
pair of legs which could be
even termed promising in
shape.”

A large number of half-
bred Chinese children attend
Brisbane Normal School.

A story of family jars was brought to a
successful ending recently by the suicide, in a
suburban park, of an elderly lady, the principal
character in the story. Her husband had died
intestate just previously, leaving <£00,000; one-
third of which, in accordance with the law, went
to the widow, and the remainder to the next-of-
kin—two nieces, cordially disliked by the old lady.
This so preyed upon her that she eventually lost
her reason, and one morning early she went out
into the park and suicided. The strange sequel
is that, as the vitriolic old dame herself died with-
out leaving a will, the whole of her money also
goes to the enrichment of the two nieces.

Martin Bros., the Adelaide drapers who
intend on March 9 to break through the agree-
ment to clcse at six on Saturday nights, are
urging the Early Closing Association to agitate
for an Act to compel all shop-keepers to shut up
at the time named, and they even subscribe <£lo
10s. to a fund to be used in preliminary work with
this object.
« « «
Adelaide drapers, who have conceded
Saturday night closing to their assistants, com-
plain that their employes, when they leave to
start businesses on their own account, in city or
suburbs, almost invariably keep open for hours
later than the shops with which they are com-
peting. An ex-treasurer of the Early Closing
Association is one of the most inveterate sinners
in this respect, his establishment, which is in the
principal street, being in full swing until 10 or 11
o’clock. Almost the whole of its trade after
6 p.rn. is, sad to relate, drawn from mechanics,
laborers and their wives.
* # *
A certain S.A. country solicitor lately
advised a client that he had a first-class case,
and certain expensive proceedings were started
accordingly. A little later he found that his read-
ing of the law was erroneous, and the client reluc-
tantly agreed to compromise and pay a heavy bill
for law charges. He gave his cheque to the
solicitor, and the solicitor gave his own cheque to the
legal opponent. But just then the client’s banker
sent him an intimation that the bank had stopped
his overdraft as it couldn’t see its way to find
money to be wasted in law, and the cheque was
dishonoured. The lawyer’s banker, on getting
back the document, stopped payment of the
lawyer’s own cheque. Then the holder sued the
lawyer for the amount, and the distracted lawyer
sued his client, and the client threatens to sue him
on the ground that his erroneous advice had been
the direct cause of the stoppage of his credit,
and had brought him to ruin. The Bulletin’s
opinion is that it is a pity this sort of thing doesn’t
happen a good deal oftener.

more and more mixed. On a recent excursion to
the West Coast Sounds, the high-toned crowd in
the saloon included 19 spielers and seven painted
ladies with gold-washed hair and badly-cracked
reputations.

When the sister of Chief Secretary Gordon
was appointed overseer of night-nurses at Adelaide
Hospital, a senior nurse, possessing all the needful
qualifications, was passed over by the Board on
the sole ground that she had once been a domestic
servant. 44 How could we take instructions from
her?” haughtily asked a probationer, the daughter
of an influential doctor. “Why, she was once a
servant in our house ! ”

It is definitely known in Adelaide that the
British Government intends to appoint a successor
to Lord Kin tore at the end of April, when his
term of office expires. Mr. Way’s reign as Lieut.-
Gov. will therefore only last about three months.
The Advertiser’s London correspondent (who
first informed Australia of the probable appoint-
ment of Lord Brassey to Victoria) affirms that the
S.A. vacancy will be filled by Mr. R. C. Munro-
Ferguson, M.P. for Leith, a junior member of the
Rosebery Ministry, and a son-in-law of the Mar-
quis of Dufferin, Ambassador to France.

In Adelaide they are prosecuting the
“Madams” and “Professors.” By the way,

# • *
Alan Fergusson, sou of Sir James of that
ilk, who h now doing 12 months in gaol for setting
fire to the school where he was supposed to be
educated, is said to be a victim of football.
He was a weakly and timid, but reasonably clever,
lad, and was a successful student in his pre-foot-
Iball period. Football was compulsory at Glenal-
rmond, on the beautifully British idea that a feeble
youth could be rendered physically strong by being
1 bashed around in a “ scrum,” and this unlucky
weakling took a hand in the game against his will,
■and w T as knocked insensible almost as a matter of
'course. Thereafter he became an erratic semi-
lunatic who was incapable of learning anything,
and who muttered to himself and saw visions.
A cheerful sermon on Nebuchadnezzar’s fiery
furnace is said to have finally sent him off the
small remains of his head, and he joyously set the
college on fire—after informing some of his school-
fellows of his intention—by way of having a
Nebuchadnezzar’s furnace of his own. Result, as
aforesaid, 12 months in gaol to recuperate his
shattered intelligence.

THE WORM TURNS.
THE THORN IN HIS FLESH : “ You miserable worm! Drink, I say,
Drink, with a capital D, is the cause of all your troubles.”
The Worm: “Alas! it’s too true. The only time that I was ever
drunk was when I asked you to marry me, and I got drunk
then (ass that I was) to screw up my courage ! ”
There is an honorable rivalry between
Melbourne and Sydney. Melbourne achieves a
charcoal-pan-suffocation horror, in which a poor,
starved, middle-aged woman murders her mother
and kills herself ; then Sydney effervesces with an
alleged double-murder attempt of a unique sort.
The Melbourne tragedy was after the French pat-
tern ; the alleged attempted Sydney crime was in
the Italian style. A money-lender is accused of
having wanted to hire a horse-trainer to sand bag
an 18-year-old girl and throw her into a waterhole,
after which he was to assassinate her elderly male
quasi-guardian according to any convenient
method that might present itself. By these deaths
the usurer, it is averred, expected to make several
hundreds of pounds and to be able to afford his
hired bravo enough to buy a partnership in a
condiment-making business. Gruesome humor !
First cold-meat, then condiment-making !

of uncertain age who is thrown out on it
to earn her own livelihood. The latest and
most pathetic of Melbourne suicides is a new
evidence of this very old truism. The
people involved were a mother and daughter

previously. They had been entitled to some

people of Melbourne grew so hard-up that no one

wanted to learn music any longer. 1 hen they got

their furniture. Cohenstein, so far as appears
from the evidence, was tolerably forbearing,
but at last he announced his intention of
seizing the sticks, “including an old-fashioned

mother and daughter got some small assistance
from a society for the aid of decayed gentlewomen
and tried to earn a living by such hopeless devices
as making lollies for sale in the market. Finally
a gruesome stench issuing from the house attracted

daughter, lying on the floor beside her.

846 Macquarie-stteet North, Sydney,
February 12, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The many and alluring advts. thrown out by
the matrimonial agencies which find you bride,
bell, book, and candle for half-a-crown, strictly in
advance, evidently don’t carry into the back-
blocks, or else a Govt. Labor Bureau wouldn’t
have raised the following peremptory demand,
which I copy verbatim :
Kindly forward me at once, to Blank Town, a woman, not
over 25:yeans, must be good-looking, a good housekeeper and
a good cook. If she suits me on arrival 1 will make her
ray wife. She must be healthy, as lam myself very
wrong and hearty. You will oblige me very much by
attending to this matter.
“Kindly forward at once ”is excellent. Romeo
himself couldn’t be in a greater hurry to clasp his
Juliet than is the Blank Town bachelor to hand
over his hearth and home to the keeping of % per-
fectly strange woman, though the most grisly part
of the affair lies in his sending his sharp order for
one wife, carriage paid, to a Labor Bureau!
Meanwhile, the astonished Department has serious
thoughts of laying in a few of the latest patterns
in brides.
Mayoress Lees—Sydney’s just-fledged Civic
Potentatess—made her debut at Sydney Town
Hall the other day, when a hurtling mob attended
liwr first reception, despite the fact that the
weather had got its goloshes on. I sincerely
trust, by the. way, that the new people aren’t
going to be Mother Carey’s chickens, like the
Burdekins, whose festive fixtures were always the
cue for deluges. Fortunately, there never is
weather of any sort under shelter, so the big hall,
clothed, as in a wedding garment, with palms,
flags and ferns, looked its very boskiest, and, if
Wiegand (at the organ) had hired somebody to
sit on the soft stop, there wouldn’t have been a
flaw anywhere. If the same unmuzzled roar of
melody is to obtain at subsequent civic
people had better learn to talk on their fingers, or
fill their pockets with conversation-lollies.
From a picturesque point of view, the new
Mayor and Mayoress are quite satisfactory. Mrs.
Lees—a small, amiable-looking Mayoress, with an
airy manner—received the heaviest throng, which
sided, ambled, trotted and bolted in, each after
his kind (why do people look all hands and feet
at such times?) as if it was individually her
dearest friend ; and wore the harmonious colors of
the rosy cockatoo, with a small, becoming bonnet
and a shower posy of pink roses. The very latest
out in Mayors—a fair-haired, pleasant-spoken Mr.
Mayor, be it said —sported the “ vermin
trimmed robes which seem to have the unhappy
knack of fitting each potentate as badly as they
did his predecessor, and the mayoral connubial
trophies, as one might say, were staged as usual
within the silken ropes with plenty of gape-seed
growing around. Miss Lees, a fair-haired and
fresh-looking damsel, wore a cream fichu topped
off by a pretty perishable sort of hat, and her
younger sister was similarly arrayed. Altogether
the mayoral party made a very pleasant group,
and maintained its composure admirably under
the rude and basilisk stare of a seore or so of per-
sons who drew their cha : rs up opposite and glared
goggle-eyed like so many hypnotists.
One of the best-dressed women at the reception
was a “ military ” matron, who cut a very taking
figure indeed in a black mirror silk elegantly
picked out with butter-color lace, a small bonnet,
and her fair hair d la Cadogan ; but the dressing
was very good on the whole, considering the
weather and the fact that Tom, Dick, and
Harry were all there. The oddest spectacle
was afforded by two young women in bright
yellow dresses, who stood up against the buffet
with their skirts turned up, linings-out, as if for a
washing bout, so that no foundered cups of tea
should spoil their garish beauties, but it made
them look as if they were wading in refreshments,
which possibly, was the case. Another unusual
spectacle was afforded by a not exactly sylph-like
he-mm-i lian plaintively asking a lady to pinch him
round the waist and tell him if his corsets were
too tight. It seems that he takes 52 inches, but
only after a great strain on his stay-laces, and he
seemed so anxious, to get her experienced opinion
as to whether he girded or not! Man really has a
frivolous soul —he would like frills round his
knickers only he has the monkey cunning to con-
ceal his vanities. For instance, I have heard of a
Sydney youth who wears stays, and simply won’t
look at them unless they are made of rainbow
coutille !
There was a most unseemly mob round the visi-
tors’-b®ok at the Mayoral Reception people
elbowing and pushing and scrunching to write
their names in order to get invited to all the civic
outbreaks that may hap. There was even—
mirabile dictu! —a more desperate struggle round
the pan and ink than around the cakes and ale—
this because pen and ink in the present meant
eakejs and ale in the future. Though, let me
remark, there wasn’t any real ala Like one of
Ids predecessors. Mayor Lees never looks upon the
wine, nor does he put temptation in the way of
his guests. A clear-headed rbgimeof milk and soda
has arrived in the marble halls that are “ taken but
surrendered.” But I have no doubt the incorrupt-
ible Town Hall youths will still keep fiery spirits
behind the almanac in the bad old style.
Talking about those stupid things, visitors
books, which are only placed in the halls of the
Great to give a literary finish to the scenery and
keep the haberdasher in affluence owing to ink-
spots on your gloves, there is a new fashion in
vogue at Admiralty House. When a person goes
to sign his name in the book the private-secretary
is always in the room—so that he will know at
least one member of the family when cards go out
for a State ball, I suppose. But this little extra
formality has frightened away a number of people
who would gaily fioek in when it was only a
question of facing a flunkey or two, and it has
also the much-desired effect of “ putting the skid
on ” the obliging friend who amiably signs not
only for herself and family but for sisters, cousins
and aunts as well, making quite a literary achieve-
ment of it. Under the calm gaze of the private
sec. a man (or woman) hardly feels equal to in-
scribing a full page of names and addresses. He
only longs to put “John Smith, his x mark,” and
flee into the outer air.
Bland Holt’s mascotte drama, “Span of Life,”
at Sydney Royal, well stands seeing a second
time, the only monotonous thing about going twice
being that the rush for seats was exactly the same
as at first. But it is a soothing thing to walk
into a playhouse clothed and in one’s right mind
(hats allowed in the circle), and men especially
bless the ukase that absolves them from shiny
shirt fronts at 8 p.m. I detected no real changes
in the “ Span ” after a seven weeks’run. Hilda
Spong’s rings seem a little more numerous (Hilda
dotes on jewellery), and sprightly Mrs. Holt plays
Shrove_ with even more relish than at first, but
otherwise the brilliant spectacle appears as
it did in the days of its youth. It is getting a
middle-aged spectacle now, and the only fear that
haunts the ever-popnlar Bland Holt is that the
expensive camel will die of old age on the premises
before he can get what sporting men call a pull on
the play. If ever a drama bolted with a manager,
“ The Span of Life ’’ haabolted with Bland Holt.
People who encourage brotherly love to con-
tinue between Church and Stage ought to be
delighted with the latest phase of that sort of
thing observable in the tight little island. Kowal-
ski and “Pussy” Poussard, when in Tas., gave
a very thinly-attended concert, somewhere or
other, and a day or so afterwards met the local
Father O Flynn (also a newspaper-editor, be it
said), to whom they confided their little trouble.
Hisßeverenceheard all aboutthe “beggarlyarray;’’
but, to their astonishment, instead of deploring
the want of culchaw in his particular dirt-heap
and passing the artists on to the next stopping-
place, he earnestly advised them to give a sacred
concert the following Sunday night.
They obediently announced a sacred concert,
and, on the same Sabbath eve, Father O’EJynn
stood up—at the end of his service—and advised
his congregation to go at once and hear the two
great artists round the corner. “It reflected little
credit on the community,” he remarked, “that
such refined and ennobling music had so far met
with such scant patronage. Music elevated the
soul of man, and producing it and listening
to it were equally an act of religious devotion.”
He advised them all to go round at once and
attend the concert, which would not occupy more
than an hour and a half. And they did go as one
individual, until the room was S. R. O.
That is probably the most promising start ever
taken by the lukewarm Church and Stage Guild.
Soon we will have clerics—when the collection
gets shy—boldly announcing from the pulpit that
“ Brother Snap—one of the finest actors it has
pleased the Lord to prosper—is playing Hamlet
even now, my dearly beloved brethren, only four
doors round the corner. And oh ! those among
3'ou who have neglected to see the wonders of
Nature, as displayed by that pure-minded maiden,
Miss Flossie Fewclothes, thereby withhold your
admiration from one of the noblest works of the
Almighty ! ” One can see it all coming. How
Brutus Snap will get even for the advt. by dress-
ing the supes. up in silk hats and quaker bonnets
and sending them to listen, open-mouthed with ad-
miration, to the parson s discourse, putting silver
in the plate meanwhile.
A distinctly amusing incident transpired at a
Sydney post-office the other day, when a cheerful
old lady came in and asked if there were any
letters for Mrs. Asterisk? Two were forthcoming.
“ And now, are there any for Mrs. Dash?” Mrs,
Dash panned out one. “ Now, please, see if there
are any for Mrs. Blank?” But the clerk jibbed
at that. “ All these people ought to apply for
their own letters, madam,” he said, in his best
official manner. “I really cannot—” but the old
lady cut him short with “Well, and ain’t they
applyin’ ? You see, I’ve been married three times,
young man, and I never know what name they’ll
send to !”
I have just heard about a rich society leader
who gave the following reply to an unfortunate
relative who had written to her asking for the loan
of one or two pounds to pay the funeral expenses
of a dead infant. “ I will not lend you the money,
as I consider it wilful waste. What you should
do is to make a box and bury the child in the gar-
den." What a magnificent baby-farmer was lost
to the world when that woman’s parents struck
it rich !
“ My attention has been drawn ” to a statement
in a Melbourne paper which cr.etiits me with having
a book on the stocks, and thereby making the
third (Third ! when every Austral Salonite is
raking Hfeaven and earth for people and ideas to
go into her seven-leagued story !) Australian jour-
naliste about to seek fame as a novelist. As it
happens, I haven’t the faintest idea of doing any
such thing. The fame that has so far rewarded
the Australian pen-lady, when she sandwiches the
utterance of her bursting soul between paper
covers and charges 2s. fid. for the result with a
heroic disdain of the fatal “thripenny baskets”
outside the booksellers, does not encourage
one to go and do likewise. Most lady-
journalist literature reminds one of the good
old editor to whom a poet offered his burning soul
on the half-shell, as it were. “No,” said the wise
old man, “ I would neither publish nor pay for it
—but our publishers will do you a very nice
volume in the style of the language of flowers for
a nominal sum if you really must appear in
print.”
The latest Sydney fad is to carry poor pussy
round the block. Travellers to Manly, the other
day, were greatly amused by the appearance of a
lady dressed in white with a blue sash and carry-
ing a large white Thomas cat with a blue eollar.
Whenever I see a woman aggressively hugging in
public a dumb animal that can’t hit back I
always wonder why she doesn’t start a nursery
and let her feelings loose on the human race?
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
George Giffen has made little out of cricket.
He has been in Adelaide G. P.O. for over 15 years, and is
now getting £lBO a year, whilst officials who joined the
department years after he did have been promoted over
his head. Giffen would, by this time, have earned a com-
petency had he been in England. Look at Grace, Walter
Reed {amateurs), and all the leading professionals.
• ♦ * *
When a N.S.W. Ministerial party was flying
around Wyalong, right in the middle of the worst part of
a track which seemed to run from nowhere to nowhere,
without a house or tent in sight, in the very bareness of
desolation, a gaunt, briek-oomplexioned, scarecrow of a
man suddenly shot into sight—also from nowhere—and
said : “Svy, mister, how many did George Giffen make in
the larst test-match ? ”
» • ♦ *
The Adelaide Oval wickets, “ the finest in the
world,” were prepared by Jesse Hide, the Sussex pro ,
who came out under engagement for five years to S. A.O.A.
Hide used to prospect the Adelaide ranges for likely soil,
and many soils were tried before he was satisfied. Adel-
aide wickets are wonderfully durable, although they
quickly cut up after bad weather. After S. A. had defeated
England the wicket was almost perfect, although over
1200 runs had been scored on it. Oheokett, the present
curator, was with Hide for years before he left again for
England to play for Sussex.
♦ « » •
Inasmuch as the final test-match in Melbourne
will clash somewhat with the V.R.O. Autumn Meeting it
is suggested that the M.C.C. ought to change their dates.
But. the cricket will'bring a lot of visitors to Melbourne,
and most of these will “do ” the races on the two last
days of the meeting under any circumstances. More-
over, the match is not unlikely to be finished by the Tues-
day, when the Australian Cup comes off.
* ♦ ♦ *
None of the Englishmen brought less than six
bats to Australia for the tour.
• » * »
The Sunday break in cricket matches, which is
the result of gate-money arrangements, leads to a lot of
“playing for the weather.” Such great changes may
come o’er the spirit of a wicket between Saturday and
Monday, that a captain winning the toss on Friday seems
bound to think of how he would like the game to stand
three days later, if there is to be rain in the meantime.
When Stoddart sent the Australians in to bat, the other
Friday, he was presumably reckoning that they would be
nearly through their second innings by Monday, when his
own men would have a fair go apiece under improved
batting conditions.
* ♦ » •
The Englishmen just about lost the last Sydney
match by missing the early chances to catch Graham.
They might possibly have topped the Australians’ first
innings on the Friday, but they didn’t. That is a fair
answer to the complaint that Australia had all thfe luck in
getting first innings after losing the toss.
* » » »
Only one English Eleven (Lord Sheffield’s) has
been beaten on the M. 0.0. ground in the last eleven years.
If Stoddart and Co. can prime their hearts with this
encouraging fact they may win the rubber. But it begins
to look as though the Australian fielding, trying weather,
and injudicious beer - drinking, have combined to
demoralise the Britishers.
* * • »
B. B. Cooper, who used to play with the Melb.
Cricket Club, came from England, with his peculiar leg-
stroke, much imitated. He had a knack of lifting his leg.
Better than some Englishmen’s knack of lifting their
little finger.
• * * ♦
The veteran Longmore, M.P., fulminates against
Vie. cricket enthusiasm at a time when the country is in
such sore straits. Lotigraore seems unaware that A. E.
Trott has clean bowled the depression.
* * * *
The sting of the Australian Eleven at Adelaide
and Sydney was in its tail. There is no sting, and only
an occasional display of stamina, in the Englishmen’s tail.
• * ♦ ♦
Leaving out of account his “double century*
score against Victoria, the Lancashire amateur, Maclaren,
has been one of the failures of the English team. Nothing
great was expected of Humphreys, Gay, Ford and
Fhillipson on their English form, and the two wicket-
keepers have done themselves justice. Brockwell and
Richardson are disappointments rather than failures.
♦ * * *
The Englishmen freely admit that the first test-
match was won for them by the wicket; but they point
out that in the second test-match they had the worst of
the wicket, and they won. In the third they had the
worst of the wicket, but A. E. Trott bowled splendidly
and they played badly. The fourth test-match they say
they deserved to lose because of the catches they dropped
in the Australians’ first innings, and they claim that then,
too, they had by far the worst of the wicket. The Eng-
lishmen say that although their downfall was due to the
wicket, the Australian bowlers did more upon it than
their bowlers could have done.
* * * *
In the interprovincial match, N.S.W. v. Queens-
land, three of the Queensland wickets fell in the first
innings for 93 ; the remaining seven put on only 13.
M‘Kibben got five wickets for 19 ; Iredale, three for 1.
When, ten years ago, Iredale first played senior cricket
in Sydney, he used to bowl for the now extinct Albert
Club. The Alberts just about that time were a good
club ; their crack bowler was S. W. Kirke, who has bowled
well this season for the Amiidale eighteen against the
Englishmen. Kirke promised to develop into an inter-
provincial howler, but nine years ago the Sydney Survey
Department, of which he is an officer, sent him up
country. He has been up-country ever since, and is now
one of the chiefs of Armidale office.
* * * «
Last week the N.S.W. Railway-Commissioners
gave the English team of cricketers, and a few Sydneyites,
who are in no way identified with the game, a picnic at
the Hawkesbury River, but, strange to say, members of
the Australian representative team were “not in it.”
Eddy naturally “cottons” to his countrymen, but
“Charley” Oliver, himself an old interprovincial man,
might well have stuck out for the inclusion of Ms little lot.
It was a “ slap-up ” affair, the lee-scuppers fairly running
with “ fizz,” the cost of which, no doubt, came but of the
privy purse of the Commissioners ; but they do not own
the engine, or the palace dining-car, or the army of
waiters, and outsiders are fools enough to think that Aus-
tralians might now and then, on their own railways, come
in for some of the good things which invariably fall to the
lot of the “blasted Britisher.”
There is much discussion going l on as to who is
to fill Moses’place in the final teshmitch, as it is pre-
Burned that he will not be picked again. The choice
appears to He between C. MoLeod and Sid Callaway.
Eady, the tall Tasmanian, who twice topped the hundred
against Victoria, is also spoken of. He, however, has had
no chance of playing against really good bowling ; where-
as both McLeod and Callaway have performed creditably
against the English bowling, besides being in fine bowling
form themselves.

When the next Australian team visits England
Richardson promises them a w arm time. “ I'd like to

Two or three of the Englishmen did not expect
the total to be more than 75 each innings in the test-
match, This was Moses’ opinion too.
♦ » * *
The Englishmen deny that in the first test-
match they batted like wooden dolls. Richardson says
that the w ieket was the worst he ever played on ; more-
over, that an English cricketer doesn’t know what a
sticky wicket means until he visits Australia.
* * » »
Richardson (this is his first visit to Australia)
says that the only remarkable feature of Australian
cricket is the fielding. It is here that the English are at
such a marked disadvantage. “ The Aiistialians all field
well ; and even the country teams field better than we do.
Look at the catches we missed off Graham.”
* • * »
The Sydney ground is considered by the Eng-
lishmen to be the best fielding ground in the world.
• * » *
“ Mick ” Roach, the coming Melb. cricketer
and footballer, lias but two fingers and a thumb on the
right hand, yet bowls a good, fast, curly ball.
* • ♦ »
The national game of football in Victoria. At
the end of last season a leading supporter of the dark
blues (Carlton) exclaimed with tears in his eyes, ‘ How
the devil could you expect them to kick goals? The poor
wretches had no boots, and we (the club) had no money
to buy them. The men who used to stand to the lads
have gone over to the Premiers.”
* * ♦ *
The Fijian cricketers touring in Maoriland
lost their first match at Auckland by an innings and 152
runs. Apropos of this, the Samoans are enthusiastic
cricketers. They play 70 a-side and an average inni gg
lasts a fortnight. Some years ago the Samoan King
Malietoa was reckoned a crack wicket-keeper.

Vanitas, Malolo, Ganmain, J 5, Eortunatus,
Erl King, and Bradford have all been supported for the
Newmarket Handicap, but how seldom do early fancies
win big events? Gaillardia has not as yet been mentioned
in the betting. She is in the same stable as Malolo and
Tullamore, and if she satisfies the astute Ike Foulsham
she should nearly win, as last year she was very unlucky
to tumble up against two such smashers as Hova and
Bruin.

Churchill (Bst. 41b.) in ’9O, and Wild Rose (Bst.
lib.) in ’92, won the Newmarket Handicap, each of them
being by Newminster out of Primrose. Eureka, an own
brother to these two sprinters, is this year weighted with
Sst. 21b., and that he is fleet of foot may be gathered from
the fact of his winning the Lawn Handicap (six furlongs)
at Fleming ton in July, ’93; since that time he has been
“resting.” Hs is now well, and will in all probability
represent the pow erful St. Albans stable.

year-olds.

except a certain five-furlong race once at Mentone.

GREAT SHIPMENT OP
CAMELS!
JUST ARRIVED.
Now on View near JPevth,
Pack & Biding Camels of the best Breed,
KHARAN,
From
BELUCHISTAN, and
AFGHANISTAN.
Most reliable for carrying and travelling purposes,
unsurpassed by any hitherto imported from India
—real beauties.
These camels are now at Lime Cliff Paddeok,
about 2J miles from Subkco and 3 miles from
Claremont, where they will remain for three
weeks to undergo the necessary quarantine regu-
lations.
They are undergoing one month’s quarantine,
during which period they will be fed on good,
strengthening food, and no expense will be spared.
These are not Calcutta camels —or, rather, those
that are known as such—but the real Hill breed ;
accustomed to feed on bushes and grass identical
with those of Western Australia; as only used by
the Army Commissariat Department of India,
consequently they can be thoroughly recom-
mended. They are dfreotly shipped from Kurra-
chee, the nearest seaport to Kharan, Baluchistan
and Afghanistan.
The s.s. Claverley, a first-class steamer-, giving
comfortable accommodation to 400 camels, was
chartered for the special purpose of bringing from
Kurraohee these 320 camels. All these earn els are
in good condition, as we have taken great trouble,
no expense having been spared by Messrs. D. and
J. Fowler on our behalf.

invited to deal at once ,

notice.

N. B.—As these camels are from a cool part of
the East, they will by the time they leave quaran-
tine be in magnificent condition. I insert this as
Indian camels are considered useless by some of
the judges in Coolgardie. This may apply to the
Calcutta camels, but cannot be taken as applying
to Kharan camels.

Re the annual gift by host Reynolds, of Mel-
bourne of a 50 guinea whip to the winning jockey of
Melbourne Cup, more than one of these whips has been
stripped and sold for old gold. One was lately in pawn
in a pub, for a beer-score, and several of the others are not
accounted for.

When “ Bravo” Jones engaged lyimey as first
mount he made him shave off his moustache and ride in
bag trousers. English^ you
Australia is going to be made very weary of the
Carbine sale, we’re thinking. A Melb. paper says that
the higher prices paid for several English stallions “ are
not to"he compared with the legitimate sum paid by the
Duke of Portland for ‘Old Jack.’” For some unknown
reason a peculiar legitimacy attaches to the Carbine deal.
Carbine will find himself in unsociable company
at the Portland stud. St. Simon and some of the other
distinguished mashers are said to have vile tempers.
A Melb. Herald correspondent suggests that,
as Oaibine is going off, he should be placed on exhibition
for a few days at Is. admission, as, no doubt, thousands
will be anxious to see him.
* * * •
“ The sale of Carbine to such a sportsman as
His Grace of Portland,” says the Argus, “is a splendid
advertisement for Australia.” Poor Australia! dragged
into fame at the heels of an entire 1
* * * *
Day stud-groom to Portland, has left England
and will accompany Carbine to his new home. It is safe
to bet that the first thing Day does to the noble brute
will be to bang his tail in real “ old Hmglish ’ style, to
match his own shaven upper lip, the badge of meniality
in that land of the free. If ever the writer sees a portrait
of Carbine in the English sporting papers with a ohopped-
off tail making him look like a heavy-rumped hack, he’ll
go straight “ Ome ” with the sole end and aim of murder-
ing the perpetrator of the horrid outrage.
St. Simon, Ayrshire, Donovan and Carbine,
now all belong to the lucky Duke of Portland.

There is some talk of increasing the Caulfield
Cup stake, which already stands at 2000 sovs. Better to
leave well alone. The Caulfield 1| mile-r-ace will never
equal the Melb. Cup in popularity, and if the V.A.T.C.
stewards were to get a much bigger crowd than they had
last year they wouldn’t be able to accommodate it
satisfactorily.

A new racecourse, called Rosebery Park, has
been laid down at Botany Road, Sydney. Competition
mav be the life of trade, but it is the death of sport.
J » » ♦ »
A few lucky ones at a recent Melb. Guvmentouse
function were treated to a gratis one-round contest with
the raw ’uns under the very portico of the hallowed
palace. As the carriages were departing at the conclusion
of the festivities ome of the lordly flunkeys, in an excess of
zeal, slammed the door of his vehicle on the ankle of the
youthful scion of a well-known sporting family, where-
upon the latter hopped out and dealt the offending one
“a slough,” almost in the presence of vice-royalty itself.
As there were no beefeaters or headsmen knocking about,
the profaner of the temple of loyalty was not dragged
away to a fearful doom, but was promptly plugged by.the
man in the plush pants. He responded by filling the
powdered-haired one’s eye, and for the next three minutes
the contending parties provided a “ real lively go.” At
length the guest landed a beautiful uppercut on the point
of the menial’s jaw, and sent Him out, amid the tears of
fashionable society. Whether the successful scrapper is
to be prosecuted for treason or not is not yet known.
r » * » *
George Giffen’s sisters play cricket well, but
they refuse to play in public.
* * ♦ •
What a farce—the rule which is supposed to
stop jockeys from betting 1 The lads have their own
especial punter who puts their little bit on their fancy.
On Saturday they picked on Olive for the Moorefleld
Stewards Mile, and a nice win they had at tens to one.
No rule can be framed which would stop jockeys
from betting, and it is a moot question whether it would
not be better to allow them to wager openly.

Apropos the 13,000 guineas given for Carbine,
the English record price, a few years ago, was £12,250 for
Blair Athol. Tneti Doncaster changed hands for £14,000,
though owner Saville was alleged to have refused £15,000
for Cremorne. Of late years these figures are put in the
•hade by the amounts given for Ormonde, St. Blatze,
Common, &e. It is said that £15,000 was offered and
refn-ed for Matchbox, second in last year’s Derby. The
appreciation of gold does not hold in the case.

likeness of Joe Thompson, who has kept his hair on
wonderfully and looks hearty. Joseph came out a
“skinner” with'his double on the Autumn Handicaps,
which is said to have f und vast favor with the English
magnates from Royalty downwards. Joe is credited with
some big wins. Rusticus’ Leicester Handicap is said to
have been worth £30,000 to him ; and £20,000 he is re-
ported to have bagged by Indian Queen’s win.

Coolgardie ice-works have started—a god-
lend to the many local fever-patients.
Byrnes, head of N.Y. police, who has just
resigned owing to disclosures made before the
Police Commission, had to admit that he was
worth a third of a million dollars. He ascribed
lus wealth to investments kindly made for him by
old Coin me dore Vanderbilt and Jay Gould, long
since dead. And dead men t 11 no tales.
At Warren, N.S. W., one Murphy, who was
in custody, used words that “annoyed the
woman” at the police barracks. So thepoliceman—
not having a Gould “gag,” it is presumed —“ leg-
ironed ” the drunk, chained him to a post at the
barracks stables, and hft him there. The post
was rotten and fell away, and Murphy walked off
to finish his drunk. Thereupon the police re-
arrested him, and charged him with stealing the
leg-irons ! The Warren Dogberry committed him
for trial for “ escaping.” Judge Coffey very per-
tinently animadverted on the action of the police,
but, all the same, his Honor sentenced Murphy to
one week’s imprisonment.

He was a stranger, and late at night
cabbed it to the railway-station. On arrival he
tendered the legal Bob-Cab-Smith shilling to the
jarvey, who, after turning it over in his hand,
gave it back, saying it was a sovereign his ’Anner
was giving him in mistake. Whereupon he handed
Jarvey 10s. with the fine, old wheeze—“So, after
all, my good man, you see honesty is the best
policy.” Next day he found the sov. to be a
gilded farthing—in fact, a fishmonger from whom
he had bought a quart of prawns talked of hand-
ing him over to the police. Truly a sovereign
remedy for a “ near ” fare.

The other day, in Sydney, a man of nearly
90 committed suicide, and a woman of over 80
fractured her skull while walking in her sleep.
You never can tell till the numbers are up.
“Man’s absurd tyranny in the past has
compelled woman to be one of two things—dull or
devil,” says Le Gallienne. Now, every woman
indignantly resents the imputation of being dull.
London Times, in a leader on the death of
ex-Chief Justice Alfred Stephen, writes of the
“ Supreme Court of Australasia being as free of
reproach and as trusted by the people as the High
Court in London.” “The Supreme Court of
Australasia 1 ” Let us federate !
The cleanliness of the Afghans is too much
for Coolgardie. They have been known to bathe
in the rock-holes, and it was through something
like this that one was recently shot. Any man,
white or black, who would bathe in any drinking-
place within 100 miles of Coolgardie deserves to
die.
Ed. Bulletin, — Be N.S.W. Works Min-
ister Young’s son in the Govt, service. Your
informant has given you a crooked version of the
case. Young, jun., holds a temporary position in
no way connected with the Works. Having
given satisfaction to his superior officers he was
retained (still temporarily) when others were dis-
charged ; but the reason of his retention, as stated
by your correspondent, is untrue. Another thing:
Why do we hear so much of the claims of a mar-
ried, as against those of a single man ? Should a
married man, perhaps unsteady and inefficient,
take precedence of an able and industrious officer,
who (without an assured income) shows his good
sense by remaining single ?— Veritas.
A “ well-connected ” Englishman, who
arrived in this beastly country a year or two ago,
and condescended to enter the Civil Service, is a
bright example of how things happen in Aus-
tralia. For a year or so he was an obscure member
of the staff and a person of no special account.
Then he showed considerable ability by inheriting
some money and marrying the daughter of his
official chief. Next, a great effort was made to
shunt the imported one’s immediate superior, in
order to make room for his promotion, but the
other man resisted so violently that the attempt
failed. So the gilded youth was “ transferred ”
over the heads of more than a dozen of his seniors
to an important country district, and when last
heard from he was doing as well as could be
expected. Still, the great device of marrying the
boss’s daughter doesn’t always come off. Some
years ago a beautiful young man in a Govern-
ment office did a prolonged mash with the pretty
daughter of another departmental official, but,
owing to business considerations, reluctantly threw
her over and married a rather plain-looking girl,
whose father was head of the office. But a new
Government came in just about then, and shunted
the old man ; and the other official, having much
solid influence in the new Ministry, got his place.
That was over six years ago. The plain-looking
wife is decidedly ugly now, and is the mother of
four children. The Johnnie who married her has
had only one rise of salary in the six years. Any-
one else can be 15 minutes late without any ques-
tions being asked, but if he is 15 seconds behind
time a voice from the inner room demands an
explanation. Also, all the odd jobs which involve
unpaid overtime fall to his lot, and if he fails to
dot an “i”his attention is sternly called to the
matter. Marrying the boss’s daughter doesn’t
always pay a dividend.

reund the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities- a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
Httle matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or

In the matter of the application of Magnus Thompson, mortgagee under memorandum of mortgage
from Jesse Plummer, No. 174,028, dated December 24, 1890, over the whole of the land comprised
in Crown grants, vol. 237, folios 147 and 148.
WHEREAS the abovenamert mortgagee has made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other faots,
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act : Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until 22nd March, 1895, whieh date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under x him,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the prin-
cipal and interest moneys due, and all costs and e«penses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest
up to the time of such sale.

street, Sydney.
