SPECIAL ISSUE of 8750 PREFERENCE SHARES ss. on application, ss. on Allotment.
The abovementioned Prtfeience Shares rank for dividends until the whole amount of 10s. has been repaid to the
Purchasers; they will then become Ordinary Shares in the Company.
I have the honor to inform you that your Directors have, “ in accordance with the subjoined resolutions passed at
an Extraordinary General Meeting of the Company held on the 28lh of January,” decided to issue 8750 Shares. The
money raised by the sale of shares will be devoted in the first place to the purchase of land required for the railway
authorised by Act of Parliament during the present session, and on which the Government propose to expend £20,060.
This railway will place yonr mine in communication w'ith the railway system of the colony, and enable a large output
to be immediately attained. It is anticipated after the railway is completed that within six months the whole of the
amount subscribed by the purchasers of the 8750 shares will be refunded in dividends.
RESOLUTIONS :
Ist. To authorise the directors to issue 18,750 shares, or such portion of the same as may be necessary (being
portion of shares now held in reserve) as preference shares, at ten shillings per share, fully paid up to ten
shillings per share.
2nd. That such preference shares shall receive dividends, in all amounting to ten shillings per share, before
ordinary shares participate in any profit.
3rd. To give power to the Directors to issue such preference shares in payment of land required for railway and
mining purposes.
4th. Shares to be allotted on such terms as the Directors may consider in the best interests of the. Company, and
in allotting the same preference shall be given to the Shareholders.
W. P. JARVIE, Manager.
Note.— Shares will be allotted as applied for, but no preference will be given to Shareholders after seven days
from the date hereof. By order of the Board. W. P. JARVIE, Manager.
Note.— Applications received at the office of the Company, or by Messrs. J. S. BUTTERS &Co., Empire Buildings,
Collins-street, who have been appointed Brokers. W. P. JARVIE, Manager, lit) Market-street.

A subscriber to a N.S.W. country news-
paper lately sought to obtain the publication at
a reduced subscription on the ground that, since
he last paid, his wife had presented him with
twins.
“ I warn the aristocracy not to force the
people to look into the subject of taxation, not to
force them to see how they have been robbed,
and plundered, and bamboozled for ages by
them. Cobden.
Styles, the member for Williamstown
(Vic.), makes out that stoppages on the Vic.
railway lines exceed the stoppages in N.S.W.,
which has also less than half as many stations, by
2,000,000 per annum, the cost of each pause being
Bd. or 9d. This would account for an increase of
.£70,000 in working exs.
A Westraliau journal says the aboriginals
who kill the squatters’ stock over there “ show
exactly the same patriotic spirit as Tell and
Washington displayed.” It is quite possible that
100 years hence, when the blacks are all dead and
people have forgotten their ugly faces and evil-
smelling skins, new Froudes and Carlyles will
write stately histories in the vein of that Wes-
tralian paper.

The well-known Turkish Baths in Bourke-
street, Melbourne (formerly Hose’s) have passtd
into the hands of M’Manus Bros., two young men
who have been connected with the establishment
under former management, and to whose en leav< > s
to please an exacting public doubtless much of its
popularity is due. The baths, retiring rooms,
&c., have been lavishly refitted and decorated,
and are now ready to receive a host of visituis.*
Coal evidently is beginning to boom in
Victoria. A number of visitors inspected the
Ou'ttrim Coal Mine, by special excursion-train from
Melbourne on 2nd Feb. instant, and were con-
siderably astonished to find that the seam of coal
was 4ft. 9in. thick, and thac a continual stream of
bullock-teams was conveying the coal to the rail-
way. Appearances justify the belief that this
mine will take a leading position in Australia
when the necessary railway facilities are afforded.
The Outtrim, Howitt, and British Consolidated
Coal Company (no liability) are making a special
issue of 8750 preference-shares, particulars of
which are afforded by the prospectus published
elsewhere.*
Dear Bulletin, —Some additional slang
derivations: “Charlie Forrester,” shearers’name
for an oil-stone, is distorted from Oharnwood
Forrest —the kind of stone once used, now mostly
replaced by Kansas stones. “ Burnt - grass-
country”—back-blocks name for an hotel with a
good table ; feed from burnt country being sweet
and nutritious. “Toe-rag”—larrikinese for .£l-
- from the odour thereof. Wanted—source of
“ hatter,” an alluvial miner who works alone;
“borak,” humbug, chaff (larrikinese); “kip,” the
short stick used in tossing coins; “grey,” a
spieler’s double-headed coin; “peg,” larrikinese
for shilling; “monkey,” for sheep. Who were
“Jimmy Woods” and “Jack Smithers” (latter
W. and C.Q.), who have given their names to solo
drinkers? Ever noticed how few slang names for
horse in this horsey country—“neddy,” moke,”
“alligator ” (W.Q.)—none racy of the soil, none
appropriate? —Wukry M.

Detective Nixon replaces Lomax at Melb.
G.P.O. The saturnine Nixon had a narrow
shave for his life when burglar Farrell shot him
in the pocket-book. In the pocket-book, mind
you, not in the Bible.
A most elaborate scheme came to grief a
few days ago in Sydney. The originator of it
went round a number of jewellery and other shops
and ordered goods on a large scale, to be delivered
when paid for. He further requested that the
bill should be sent to his loving father in Rock-
hampton, and in each case he wrote a letter to the
loving father explaining the circumstances and
asking the old man to send along the money.
This letter he handed over to be enclosed with the
invoice, and the shopkeepers were much impressed
by the intensely affectionate nature of the epistle.
Then the alleged son s- oms to have gone up to
Rockhampton to conduct the other end of the
correspondence; at all events, each firm received
a letter from the alleged father also written in an
enthusiastically affectionate strain, enclosing a
cheque for the amount and something over, and
directing that the surplus should be handed over
to the offspring. It was just here that the trouble
came in. Dempster, manager for Stewart Dawson
and Co., was staggered by the old man’s lavLhness
and the affectionate manner in which he addressed
the young man who was “ blowing ” his substance
in riotous jewellery. Also, he made a mental note
of the fact that fathers, even when they pay their
sons’ jewellery bills, don't generally do it in such
extreme haste, and don’t generally write almost
exactly the same hand as their sons. In view of
all these circumstances, he called in a detective
ard the rest was chaos.

Dear Bulletin, —A few notes re Cool-
gardie. It is said here that, when exemption ex-
p-res at the end of January, sufficient men will
not be locally obtainable to work the mines.
Whether that will be so, I cannot say, but here are
two illustrations of the present state of the labor-
market. Some days ago, Bayley’s Reward 00.
called for tenders for cutting a drain 40 chains
long, 4ft. wide at the top, Ift. at bottom, and l£ft.
deep. Being hard-up, I thought I would tender.
At the time appointed to meet the surveyor, no
less than 19 hungry-looking men attended. I
offered at 10s. a chain, but did not get the job. I
have since been told that it was let at Bs. The
second case is that of a “ stiff ’’dryblower, who was
lucky enough to get a job serving at the condenser
and looking after the boilers. His hours were from
6 a.m. to 10 p.m.; wages, J6l a week and tucker.
Last week a little donkey-cart was to be seen
wending its way over the ridkety paths through
the suburbs of this dusty place.- Seated therein
was the shadow of a man, the very picture of
death. “ What’s up?” enquired inquisitive way-
farers, The driver explained that the man in the
cart was' his‘ mate, and that he was taking him
from the hospital. “ From the hospital ?” was
the astonished query. “Yes, he is discharged as
cured.” Here followed a few curses and a heap
of a|>use of the hospital authorities. I went to see
the cured man, and found that he had been down
with typhoid. It is a disgrace that men should
be turned out of a hospital in such a condition.
Fortunately this man has a good mate, but. there
are many who are as poor as crows. Is it any
wonder that so many cases of relapsed fever take
place?—Queenslander.

HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Prince » f Wales.
An Abandoned Tub,
(With Apologies to Swinburne.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
In a nook of the town, neither streetway nor alley,
At a corner drear, where no larrikin bands
Spit in the dusk, or with damsels dally,
The ghost of a grog-shop silent stands.
Four crumbling walls and some woodwork waning
Enclose a waste that is smelled afar—
Not a sign or trace in their midst remaining
Of the bar.
The din of the crowd’s distant coming and going,
Mocking the moan of a mourner in grief,
Sleeps on the breast of the wild wind blowing
Over the sign of “The Golden Sheaf ” ;
The noise of the waggon, or ’bus full-freighted,
Comes never anear to this moss-grown place,
Where slim, callow youths, by potations elated,
Went the pace.
Here there was laughing of old, there was weeping,
H iply, of drinkers none ever will know—
Husbands who drank whilst their manhood was
sleeping
And wan wives, waiting, sang songs of woe ;
Johnnies, who hearkened to hoary blasphemers
Pitching lewd tales from sin-stained lips,
Or listened to touts from the turf-land of dreamers
Bringing “tips.”
Where the lean, grey cat hunts for mice on the
ashes,
Barmaids of beauty were wont to smile,
Whose eyelids, all dark at the lines of their
lashes,
Drooped over orbs that shone with guile
When a lover, snatching a red-rose token
From the summit soft of a padded breast,
Gave vows for the vows that are seldom spoken,
Save in jest.
Did true love bloom in this corner forsaken?
Is the perfume of passion still lurking nigh ?
Did a cheek turn pale when a hand was shaken
Over the counter in years gone by ?
D.d heart meet heart when the welcome clatter
Of glasses and tongues drowned a whisper low ?
Did courtship shape idols for marriage to shatter,
Long ago ?
Nothing is left—not a sign, or a story
Telling of life in. that pub., whose name
G learned bright on the glass with a golden glory
Which passed away like a hero’s fame.
Tue wild wind breathes no echo of laughter,
Laughed by the drinkers in times agone:
H ere the Spirit of Death, who shall die hereafter,
Reigns alone.
Melbourne.
E. F.
Mr. Mundella, who had to quit the Rose-
bery Ministry through being mixed up in the N.Z.
Loan and Mercantile scandal, is an utterly broken-
down man. Evidently a matter of climate.
When an Australian politician loses his political
reputation he begins to make flesh, as a rule.
It was remarked of the late John Thomp-
son (the Canadian knight who died of seeing the
Queen at Windsor Castle) that he had faltered
and rambled in his speech at the Colonial Insti-
tute, the previous evening. He must have faltered
and rambled an awful lot for it to bo noticed —at
the Hinstitute.
The Stuffing of Slattery .
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
The Burrowa (N.S.W.) election, just over, which
has resulted in the defeat of Tootney and the return
of Kenneth Mackay (the latter supported by the
retiring member, Tom Slattery, who was ban-
queted in Sydney last week), recalls an incident
which occurred when Tom Slattery aforesaid first
stood for the constituency. John Nagle Ryan
(Burrowa, ere it was joined to Murrumburrah,
consisted solely of Ryans) had previously re-
presented the electorate. He was a fat, easy-
going, absurdly hospitable local squatter, who
never opened his mouth except to put something
into it. Nevertheless, statesmanship soon proved
too exhausting an occupation for John Nagle
Ryan, who retired, and recommended “ Tom ”
Slattery as his successor. Thomas had just then
resigned the N.S. Wales Supreme Court pro-
thonotaryship owing to a dispute with auto-
crat, Parkes. As anyone who had sufficient
courage to beard H. Parkes in his den, as Slattery
had done, was sure to be regarded as a man of
grit, Burrowa was elated with its candidate, more
especially as the electors all detested Parkes and
were glad of an opportunity to show it by return-
ing to Parliament a man with such strong personal
and public reasons for opposing him. Here let an
elector of Burrowa take up the story :
“None of us up to this had either seen Mr,
Slattery or heard a description of his personal
appearance, but from the defiant stand he made
against Parkes we concluded that he must at
least be built on the lines of a spade-pressed bale
of wool. For in those days the majority of us —
the unwashed—held such extraordinary notions
of the outward and visible indications of courage,
power, ability, and wealth, that we could not con-
ceive it possible for these attributes to exist with-
out the accompaniment of a big bingey. Imbued
with these ideas we formed a most extra-
ordinary conception of our candidate’s physical
proportions. Judge our disappointment when,
instead of the adorable monster our fancy
had painted, a long, thin, hungry-looking pock-
marked lawyer, in a long, rusty black coat, put in
an appearance ! The shook was terrible.
“ On the night of the new-comer’s arrival many
of in rode into Burrowa from different parts of
the electorate to see and hear him, but chiefly to
see, for as long as he was physically fit we did not
trouble about his oratorical powers. Wj reached
Burrowa sometime after dark, and proceeded to
the hotel, where we were informed the can-
didate had put up. There we met one of
Nagle Ryan’s chief local supporters, who appeared
utterly despondent and half-full of whisky. This
we regarded as a bad omen, for when matters
went straight with our friend he was either quite
sober or dead-drunk, as he hated half-measures on
principle, and never indulged in them save when
upset and unable to carry his liquor like the
thorough gentleman he was.
“ After the usual salutations we asked him if
he ha,d seen the candidate. He replied that he
had. ‘ What do you think of him ?’ we next
enquired. ‘ What do I think of him,’ replied
Mick, ‘ what do I think of him ? Ah, boys, don’t
a*k me, don’t ask. We are done, boys, we are
done. He’ll niver, niver get in ! It is a swindle,
boys—he had better go back.’ ‘ Why, what’s the
matter?’ we enquired, ‘ isn’t he a clever, capable
and long-headed man—weren’t we told he was
one of the smartest men in Sydney ?’
“ ‘ We were, boys,’ said Mick ; ‘ and he is smart
and clever enough, and his head is all there. He
have a great head, but where be his belly ? He
hasn’t it, boys, he hasn’t it. He’s as thin as a
pole and has no more belly than a blackfellow’s
dog. He would only be wasting his time and ours
going to the polls.’ Here Mick fairly broke down
and wept.
“ ‘ But surely,’ we suggested, ‘the absence of a
big belly should not be a fatal obstacle to the can-
didate’s return. If he possesses a fluent and
logical tongue, backed up with an active and
reflecting brain, these qualities, together with a
spirit of honesty and patriotism, should carry
some little weight with the electors, and we think
we should do all we possibly can to secure his
return.’
“ ‘ Me boys,’ said Mick, pityingly, ‘ it is plain
from your remarks that you are new to politics.
A fluent tongue, a well-turned brain, honesty of
purpose and patriotism are attributes that are all
very well in their place, but they are no good in
politics. There is our last man, John Nagle
Ryan. Where was his fluent tongue and active
brain? He hadn’t them, boys. Share the only
once he spoke out loud during the time he repre-
sented us in Parliament was when he had the
rheumatics and couldn’t well walk across theflure,
and so he had to sing out in axing a man that was
sitting with the Ministry bow he liked Cassidy’s
new whisky. No, boys, John had neither a fluent
tongue nor an active brain, but he had what was
of more service to him—he had a big belly, lads.
The possession of a big belly put John Nagle
Ryan in, and the want of a big belly will keep
Tom Slattery out. For 20 years, boys, I have
been a voter in this electorate, and we have always
been represented by a fat man, and the people are
now so used to it that they won’t vote for a thin
one. In fact, they have no confidence in a man
without a big belly. But I have a plan that may
save us yet—and if you will come across with me
to Ryan’s and have a drink I will place it before
you, and, if you approve of it, you can afterwards
assist me to carry it out and make sure of the seat
for this man Slattery yet.’
“ We took our drinks and sat round the
table, anxiously waiting for Mick’s scheme
to defeat the opposition candidate and return
our own. At last the old man said: ‘Well,
boys, as I have already told you, this elec-
torate has always been represented by a fat man,
and the fatter the man and the bigger his belly the
more pronounced and decided has been his victory.
I think you will agree with me when I say that it
is utterly hopeless for our candidate to appear
before the public in his present wretched grass-fed
condition, but, fortunately for the purposes of my
plan, very few of the electors have seen him, and
the few who have are staunch supporters of
Ryan’s, so that, should they notice any change in
his appearance, they will know its purpose and be
silent on the matter. My plan is this, that we
form ourselves into a deputation to wait on the
candidate, place the circumstances of the case
before him, point out that it is useless for him to
appear before the electors as thin as he is, and
ask him to stuff himself; and if he has the in-
terests of this electorate at heart he will comply
with our request and make up a bingey on himself. ’
“ ‘But suppose he should refuse,’ we asked,
‘ what is to be done ? ’
“ ‘What is to be done? Well, then, boys,’ said
Mick, ‘if he should refuse, by jabers, we must
stuff him. There is no time for fooling now. The
opposition man has a big belly, and ours is not
going to the poll without one—not if I can help it,
so that if he should refuse to stuff, we must get
hold of him, take him down to my place, and
Jimmy, the tailor, will soon let a gusset into the
back of his garments; and with the aid of some
wool he’ll give him an appearance of respectability
which will secure the confidence of the electors.’
“We had fully arranged to put our plan into
operation when news reached us that the opposi-
tion candidate was taken seriously ill and had
retired in favor of a commission-agent who was
fully as thin as our ghost; so that, their corpora-
tions being about equal; we decided to have the
contest decided by a battle of brains, and our man
won. I suppose he hasn’t heard from that day to
this how near he was to being stuffed.”

Marshal Canrobert, one of the muddled
heroes of the Second Empire, lately died of senile
de oay at the age of 86. Military and alleged
military men furnish a large proportion of modern
patriarchs. Consider a few recent examples taken at
random : Moltke, Canrobert, Macmahon, Serrano,
Concha, Early, Beauregard, Butler, Cialdini,
Klapka, and Jovellar, the average ages of the lot
at deat !i being over 75 years. In 18.14, 32 British
generals and admirals pegged out at ages ranging
between 70 and 92, half of them being over 80.
Apparently military and naval bloodshed is a
healthy pursuit—for the men at the top of the
ree an yhow

During the last week or two the news
has arrived from half-a-dozen new quarters that
Max O’Rell proposes to take “ proceedings ”
against Sydney Truth for publishing some
alleged chapters from his last _ book, said
chapters being a great deal brighter than
anything in the book itself. Max, it seems,
calls Taylor’s well-meant little advt. an
“impudent fraud.” This violence on the
part of the little man forces The Bulletin to the
conclusion that he is only a dreary and mechanical
humorist, after all. A true humorist can at least
see a good joke when it is brought violently under
his notice ; the O’Rell can’t, unless the other
humorist gets him down and sits on him, and ex-
plains the thing at length, and won’t let him up
until he fully understands it.

The Elder’s Prayer,*
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
His soul was traffic, his honor was gold,
And men and women he bought and sold
Who lies beneath this sod ;
He did good deeds, hut even these
Were bribes to Almighty God.
—Epitaph.
Oh, Lord, I am a sinfu’ man,
I ken I stand within your ban,
Oh, thank you muokle for your plan
O’ blessed charity !
By which poor sinners a’ may stan’
In regularity !
Rioht well I ken I’ve robbed the poor,
My bank—l had to close its door;
Oh, mercy, dinna look so sour,
Will saxpence do ?
Next week, mayhap, I’ll gie ye more ;
Well, there, then—two !
For oh, guid Lord, I’m poor myael’!
My bairns maun learn to write and spell.
My wife needs many a yard and ell
O’ silken gear.
Your lowest, Lord, to dodge yon hell —
Come, noo, that’s fair!
The Sabbath, when I keep your law,
I’ll gie ye saxpence—weel, say twa —
It breaks my heart to hear them fa’,
Puir chinkin’ bodies.
Man’s hate is hard, but worst of a’
The wrath o’ God is.
Buddies, wi’ here and there a patch,
I’ll gie to any naked wretch
That on the cold ground has to stretch,
Shivering, coughing.
But, Lord, thy servant does beseech,
Don’t send them often.
What comfort, Lord, ye do proclaim—
Thot cup o’ water in your name
Thot gies a preferential claim.
I’ll tell ye what—
Send a’ the hungry, blind and lame,
I’ll gie them — thot.
And, then, ye winna, Lord, forget
That old per contra twixt us yet.
Before Sin put me in your debt,
I gave bawbees ?
Aiblins the nicht this thocht will let
Me sleep at ease.
My earthly creditors, I found,
Were glad enough and smiled all round
Wi’ two-pence-ha’penny in the pound,
And satisfied ;
But, Lord, you grind me down and down
For more beside.
Your Book-keeper’s own damning mou’
Tells o’er the items owed to you;
And a’ the weary lang nicht through
I groan and pray.
Why won’t a composition do
Wi’ time to pay ?
J. H. GRRENE.
* Probably a Melbourne elder.
Bill Somers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Bill Somers cheated me out of a nice little
fortune of some fifty or sixty thousand pounds.
Now, I never saw Bill Somers in my life; and he
never saw or heard of me, and did not gain a
single cent by the transaction. Nevertheless,
Bill Somers is the man I have to thank for the
present unsatisfactory condition of my finances.
I was managing my uncle’s station—it ought to
be mine, now. He lived in Sydney, paying only
an annual visit to the place. He was a bachelor,
and as he was a crusty and most argumentative
old dog, I used to think it was just as well we only
met occasionally.
Now, I myself am not argumentative except
when people don’t agree with me; still, one
cannot live with a Sir Oracle for long
without feeling obliged to differ from him occa-
sionally. Naturally, my uncle supposed himself
an authority on nearly every question under the
sun, and I have no doubt he persuaded himself
that he had really gone through the many experi-
ences he used to relate. He was never at a
loss for information to bolster his inventions;
for he could always quote a man he used to know
who knew all about it. That’s how I came to hear
about Bill Somers.
I was in Sydney for a few days on station busi-
ness, and was dining with my uncle. Charming
cottage and grounds (ought also to be mine).
Having finished all the “shop” we had to talk
about, the old gentleman was holding forth on
some sharp practices lately brought to light
amongst the provision - shipping merchants.
“Why,” he said, “in my time we would as
soon ” There he hesitated, and I chipped in,
jocularly, as 1 filled my glass : “Come, governor 1
you know you were never in the provision-ship-
ping line.”
My uncle was speechless for some time, whether
from rage or because he was making something
up I don’t know ; the latter, I suspect.
“No, sir,” he said slowly at last; “I was
never in the provision line, as you justly remark.
But, was not my friend, Bill Somers, one of the
leading provision-merchants in Sydney, and ”
here he almost shouted—“don’t I know all the
ins and outs of the business from him ?”
My uncle glowered at me for the rest of the
meal, and I was glad to get back to town.
I was engaged at that time to one of the nicest
girls in Sydney (who also ought to be mine). She
was a great favorite of my uncle’s ; and as I was
then fully justified in considering myself his heir,
I had not much thought for the future. I told
Kate all about my taking a rise out of the old man,
and, to my surprise, she told me that I was very
foolish. What was the good of contradicting my
uncle about such a trivial thing? This annoyed
me ; and although, as I said before, I am not at all
of an argumentative disposition, still I feel it my
duty to assert myself and not allow statements to
pass unchallenged when I know them untrue. I
told Kate something like this; but she did not
agree with me, and we parted rather coldly.
I went out to lunch at Nardoo Cottage next
day; but, as ill-luck would have it, we had not
got half-way through the meal when my uncle
a subject on which I hold strong views—
strong, simply because they are right. I like
neat well-done, and contend that is the proper
and healthy way to cook it. My uncle likes meat
underdone—raw, I call it; and most absurdly
insists that it is more nutritious when taken so.
Over this apparently insignificant subject we fell
out ; and, my uncle being somewhat bitter and
personal in his remarks, I retorted rather smartly
I can be very sarcastic when it pleases me) :
“ I suppose Bill Somers liked his meat raw ?”
I thought he was going to throw something at
me.
“ Bill Somers was not a jackanapes like you, so
he liked his food properly cooked,” he growled.
“Let me see,” I returned, reflectively, as I
opened a bottle of sodawater. “ You say he
traded down to the Cannibal Islands. I suppose
that is where he acquired the taste.”
“ Leave my house 1” shouted my u-ncle at the
top of his voice. I laughed, and remarked that I
would look rather queer appearing in the street
bareheaded, with a glass in one hand and a bottle
of soda in the other. This maddened him still
more, but I did not let it disturb me, for we had
rows like this whenever we met. Really, I must
admit that the old fellow had been very kind to
me.
When I met Kate that evening she said, “ How
can you be so silly as to keep irritating your
uncle ? He called here this afternoon, and I could
see that you have offended him very deeply. You
will go too far some day.”
This annoyed me very much. Kate evidently
meant that my conduct might make a difference
in my prospects ; and this, I consider, hardly
showed a proper appreciation of me. I knew that
my uncle had in reality far too much admiration
for my talents and ability to quarrel seriously.
In fact, I was quite sure that he respected me all
the more for my sturdy independence. Kate did
not agree with me, and made a most uncalled-for
remark to the effect that my conceit would
be the ruin of me. We parted still more coldly
than before, but I felt that Kate needed a lesson
as well as my uncle.
I had to return to the station in a day or two,
but was invited to dinner at Nardoo Cottage, to
meet a few friends of my uncle. He was a for-
giving old boy, I must say.
“Now, Harry,” said Kate, “do not go out of
your way to contradict your uncle this evening.
Remember the difference in your ages and posi-
tions, and wh.it you owe him.”
I gave Ka .e a bit of my mind in return, and
reduced her to tears before I left.
There were a bank-manager and two squatters
at dinner that evening, and the talk was mostly
“shop.” I rather flatter myself on having stated
my opinions upon stock-breeding and station-
management confidently and in a way that left an
impression. We were smoking with our wine,
and the subject of cigars came up. Now, lam as
good a judge of a cigar as any man, so, when my
uncle praised those we were smoking, I lost my
patience, for they were really nothing to brag
about.
“ They aren’t bad,” I commenced, “ but ”
Here the old gentleman snapped in : “ Con-
found your impudence ! Not bad, indeed ! You
conceited young blockhead ! do you know the
difference between a cigar and a cabbage ? ”
All the other old fogies chuckled ; and, very
naturally, I felt nettled. However, I knew how
to rub it in, so I exhaled a long puff of smoke and
drawled out: “ I presume Bill Somers selected
them.”
My uncle went purple. If apoplexy had only
carried him off just then, I should have been right.
He gasped, and then restraining his passion by a
great effort, said quite quietly, “ I have a word to
say to you later on.”
I was very facetious the rest of the evening.
Emboldened by the way I had put the old man
down, I had two or three more sly little hits about
Bill Somers, until I felt that I must not wear the
joke threadbare. That’s the best of my wit—l
always know when to stop ; I don’t run a thing to
death like most men. At a sign from my uncle, I
stayed behind when the others left.
“Henry Jamison,” he said, “you appear to
have deliberately gone ouc of your way to insult
me every time you have accepted my hospitality.
To-morrow you will return to Grimgums (the
station), and remain there until you hear from me.
Good night.”
He certainly had the last word, and as I strolled
townward I felt very wroth at the mean and
unjust accusation he had brought against me.
Just because he was too dense to understand my
perhaps rather subtle humor and certainly harm-
less chaff, he said that I “insulted” him. I
returned to Grimgums feeling very sure that my
■uncle would be extremely sorry for his conduct.
Scarcely bad I been a week on the station when I
received the following astounding epistle :
Dear Mr. Jamison.
If you look back, as I have done, you will, I am sure,
agree with me that our tempers are not sufficiently
sympathetic to hold out any prospect of happiness in the
future. I have no wish to enter into details, as I know a
little reflection will convince you that I am acting for the
best. Your uncle, who was always in favor of our en-
gagement, reluctantly admits that lam right. We will,
therefore, consider the engagement at an end, but I shall
always remain your sincere friend,
Katb Denby.
Was there ever such a shameful thing? Just
because a girl cannot have everything her own
way, she deliberately throws a man over. This
letter was enough to give me food for thought,
but I was fairly puzzled by one I received from
my uncle a few days later. In It there was the
following passage :
So through your infernal conceit and foolish want of
tact you have lost one of the best girls in the world ; but
there is one thing I can tell you: you will be the only
loser in the matter—she certainly shall not.”
Fancy ! I, conceited ! A more modest man does
not live. I may have sufficient self-respect to
estimate myself at my right value, but that is all.
Explanation of ray uncle’s remark came to me
in the form of a newspaper with a marked para-
graph soma six weeks later. My uncle and Kate
Denby were married !!!
I was so justly incensed that I resigned my
billet at once. This happened three years ago,
and the other day my uncle died—leaving a widow
and a son and heir.
I suppose the brat’s godfather was Bill Somers.
